WASHINGTON, March 7.â The War Department
expects important news from Manila within the next twenty
four hours. Word has been received from General Otis that
he will not wait for the arrival of Lawton and the troops on
the Grant and Sherman before he begins a general attack
upon the insurgents.
The advices say Otis will, either to-day or to-morrow,
make the contemplated advance upon the Filipinos with his
entire force. The arrival of the men on the transports Sena
tor and Ohio has given him enough reinforcements to hold
Manila while he uses the other troops for an aggressive cam
paign against the rebels.
It is expected in this attack Aguinaldo will be captured
and much done to end the insurrection in the island of
Luzon.

Information . was slightly wounded.
Private John Curran of the Twentieth

examined the jungie for five hundred
yards beyond our tines. No live Fili
pinos were seen, but the bodies of many
dead rebels were found where they had

enemy were killed. The village of La
Guig has been burned by the Ameri
- cans.
The natives resumed desultory firing
in the afternoon on Wheaton's and
Hale's brigades. Our infantry and ar
tillery replied and soon drove the enemy
from their position.
At the time of sending this dispatch
the Wyoming troops occupy an ad
vanced position. The insurgents opened
fire across the river from Guadaloupe
and killed Private Lovejoy of Company
C, First Washington, and wounded two
others.
The insurgents have been concentrat
ing to the east of the city and will prob
ably try an attack from that side. Their
principal object is to cut off the water
supply of Manila. The American posi
tions are strong and the attempt will
be futile. The insurgent losses have
been severe during the last few days.
The Madrid authorities have offered
Aguinaldo a ransom of $1000 each for
officers, $100 each for privates and $500
each for civilian servant prisoners. No
offer has been made to ransom priests.
Aguinaldo declares he has paid fili
busters $700,000 and has received inly
600 rifles and 2,000,000 rounds of ammu
nitJion. The filibusters say the re-'
mainder have been captured, but prob
ably they were never bought.

eral men w/re detailed to advance and

lieutenant and his party had gone
about 600 yards beyond their lines
â when it was discovered that the bear
ers of the flags were armed and that a

replied to the flag of truce.
As soon as the danger of the situa
tion was revealed, Lieutenant Koehler
and his men started to return to the

opened fire on them, but fortunately

ONE MORE DEFEAT
WILL SETTLE REBELS
WASHINGTON, March 7.â The War
Department officials were gratified to
learn through the press dispatches that
an attack had been made at San Juan
Del Monte and the insurgents routed
with only one casualty to the Amer
ican troops. Much confidence Is ex
pressed by the officials that one more
big defeat will so forcibly impress the
insurgents with the overwhelming
power and superior fighting qualities of
the American troops that they will of
fer little or no further resistance.
The transport Grant, with more than
a regiment on board, will reach Manila
in a few days. The Sherman is fol
lowing close behind her, and the Sheri
dan probably has a voyage of three
week, before her yet. With these re
inforcements General Otis' forces will
be augmented to the extent of four full
regiments of seasoned regular troops,
with a numerical strength of about
5000 officers and men. This will give
General Otis an army of nearly 35,000,
which, in the belief of officers of the
army, will be quite sufficient to cope
with any exigencies.
The following transports with their
conveying capacities will leave Manila
for San Francisco on the dates set:
Arizona, 1200. March 9; Newport. 600.
March 10: Scandia, 1300, March 11:
Morgan City, 700. March 12. The Zea
landia, with accommodations for 600
men. is in port at San Francisco, but
will not be able to sail for three weeks
because of necessary repairs. As soon
as the first named vessels arrive at
San Francisco they will be loaded with
tro-ops which are under orders to r>ro
ceed to Manila and sail with as little
delay as possible

â  ROME. March 8. â The Italian Government is fully de- â

NEW YORK, March 7.â The Herald's Washington correspondent sends
the following: Not only did Italy endeavor to ascertain what attitude
would be assumed by the United States in case of her occupation of Chinese
territory, but I learned to-day she actually asked this Government to sup
port her pretensions. The startling proposition greatly astonished the ad
ministration.
Never before has the United States been consulted by any Euro
pean power relative to its seizure of Chinese territory.
But the note of the Italian representative here is considered by the au
thorities as formal recognition of the new position in international affairs
which the United States has assumed as a result of the war with Sptin,
and of the immensity of the commercial interests of this Government la
Asia.
Secretary Hay's reply was brief and sufficiently plain to show Italy Just
how this Government felt. It distinctly and unequivocally refused to pup
port the Italian Government and stated that the United States had sever
supported or interfered in any way with the efforts of other government to.
obtain territory and "spheres of influence" in China, and would mos,. cer
tainly not do so in the case of Italy.
The tone of the note was deprecatory of Italy's intention, but no formal
protest was made and from present indications none will be. Italy's efforts
to get the support or at least passive consent of the United States die . â ;.
end with the presentation of the note and receipt of Secretary Hay's r^pl'.
The Italian Minister in Peking approached Minister Conger several *
ago and asked him to use his influence in getting China to consent â  :. â¢
Italian demand. Mr. Conger diplomatically evaded an answer, and :;
cabled to Washington. The instruction. sent to him was along the )â¢ â  - -
the" reply made her.. ' 6
While authorities' information show that Great Britain is diplor
opposing the Italian demand it is apparent to them that Great Brit;
within a very few days either distinctly reiterate her intention to r . ; , *
the integrity of China and support her open-door policy or annoi â¢
reversal.
It was stated to-day that no communication relative to the i^ctwsdfj
demands had passed between Secretary Hay and Sir Julian Pauncefr but
it would not be surprising if there had not been a discussion in Lone be
tween the American Embassy and the British Foreign Office.
The authorities are anxiously awaiting the announcement of Great Brit
ain's policy, for it is appreciated that it is of great importance to the
United States.
It was definitely announced to-day that so far as the United Stafca;is
concerned there is no intention to interfere in any way with the Italian rlt
mand or to take any step whatever, at this time at least. i j>,. ;
The administration is not even willing to use its friendly offices to open?
China's eyes to the delicate position she occupies, the idea being that it ifl
unwise for this Government to interfere in any Wky, in view of the di^icuit
problems already confronting it in Cuba. Porto Rico and the Philip
Besides the administration appears satisfied that although portions o the
maritime provinces of China are passing under the control of European
states no discriminating treatment of American citizens and their wade^f
will occur.
Diplomats best acquainted with the Chinese situation believe China vviii
not be formally divided up among the several powers. They think the-sev
eral governments will try to extend their "sphere influence" until practi
cally the entire empire is divided among them.
RIVER CHOKED
WITH CORPSES
OF THE SLAIN
Chinese Rebels Massacre Thou
sands of Men, Women
and Children.
VICTORIA, March 7.â According to
news received this evening by EL If.
S. Empress of India. China is again
disturbed by a serious insurrection.
This time it is the central provinces of
the unrestful land that are Involved.
In the province of Anhui depredations
were committed by the rebels, who. led
by Niu Suh Hsin, are over 10,000 strong.
The imperial troops, under General
Kuo. met thorn in a pitched battle on
January 23 and were defeated with
great slaughter. Hundreds were kill
ed, and their bodies, after havine been
mutilated, were thrown into the river
until, according to a correspondent of
the China Mail, the stream was like
a log-jammed crepk.
After the defeat of the imperial
troops, the victorious rebels swept on
to the cities of Kuyang and Meng
sheng, which they took after a short
siege. As soon as they passed the
walls they massacred men. women and
children and performed all manner of
revolting cruelties. They afterward
pillaged the Yamen and the residences
and store and then applied the torch to
the captured towns.
Aftor these successes the rebels
pushed on to Shachou and Katichon.
The gates of the former city were
opened by sympathizers within and
the horrors witnessed at their first two
captures were re-enacted.
Kauchon held out for some time. At
length Niu and his followers gained an
entrance to begin their slaughter. As
a revenge for his having held the city
against the rebels the unfortunate com
mandant of the garrison was butchered
PRICE FIVE CENTS.
with savage cruelty. It is said 200G
men, women and children fell in the
struggle attending the capture of the
city.
It is feared a great famine will fol
low the insurrection, for so terrified are
the natives that the crops have all been
left standing and will not n<> harvested,
The people were either killed or feared
to return to gather the harvest. Whole
villages and towns were tenantless and
in other towns who!<= streets were un
occupied, the late inhabitants having
been exterminated.
It appears that rebels and troops
alike waged a war of extermination,
neither man, woman nor child being
spared.
* A gentleman who reached Hongkong
from the vicinity of the uprising short
ly prior to the sailing of the Empress
said he saw numbers of mutilated
corpses floating down the river. Most
of the corpses were headless. They
were those of mal<=Â» and female, young
and old. He said he was sickened by
the sight of the headless body of a
woman with the headless body of her
infant secured to her back.

BURNS STILL WANTS
SECRECY TO COVER
HIS DARK SCHEMES
Afraid of the Light of Day on His
Senatorial Fight.
THE PEOPLE VS. THE RAILROAD
The Lines in the Contest Are Clearly Defined and the
Cohorts of the Corporation Stand Against
the Commonwealth.
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, March 7.â The touts are still
working under cover to obtain peti
tions from the public calling upon
members of the Legislature to settle
the deadlock by the calling of a caucus.
Burns Is unwilling to accept the decis
ion of the Republican conference that
a majority of the Republicans of the
Legislature preferred to settle the Sen
atorial contest in the open. Petitions
have been left at saloons and pool
rooms, where residents and non-resi
dents, miners and aliens have been in
vited to sign.
The followers of the Candelarian as
sume that a candidate has the right to
call a party caucus to determine his
own standing in the contest. The fai t
Is that a caucus is the right of the
party and not that of the individual.
In many States the caucus system is
not recognized at all, and where it has
been brought into use it has been used
to bind the signers to the cause of the
party's candidate. In this contest now
in progress the Republicans of the Leg
islature are confronted with a candi
date brought forward by the railrcrad
i-nmpany. Had Burns announced his in
tention before the election to become a
candidate for United States Senator the
people would have understood the
question and obtained a declaration
from the candidates on the legislative
ticket, but tne intentions of Burns and
the railroad were carefully concealed
from the people.
It is too late now to come forward
and claim a "party caucus when the
party was not consulted as to the can
didacy of Burns before the election.
In view of this condition of affairs the
Republicans of California are follow
ing the course pursued by the Repub
licans of Pennsylvania â they are light
ing the Senatorial battle o-n the Boor
if the joint convention in open day
light.
The railroad says that Burns can win
if he can get a caucus. The touts say
that he will win if he can get a caucus.
In order to deceive the unwary, he may
feign weakness in the hope of luring
men into a secret caucus. The Barnes,
Orant and Hulla men understand the
programme, but they are cautioned
against the relaxation of vigilance.
The order has gone from the yellow
building to the servants of the corpora^
tion that the Senator shall be no one
but Burns. Members of the Legisla
ture representing Los Angeles County
received telegrams to-day, asking them
to bring about the election of a United
States Senator. N<> preferences were
expressed, which leads to the belief that
there Is a sentiment in that region of
the State favorable to the election of
an able and reputable candidate from
the northern part of California. There
have been so many vigorous protests
from the south against the considera
tion of Burns that the inference is
drawn that any other candidate than
Burns would be acceptable.
Wherever the people have been con
sulted the aspirations of Burns have
been rebuked. According to his own
statement, he became a candidate
against his will to rebuke the press of
the State for criticizing his public acts.
He presents no higher claim to the of
fice than a desire for personal vindi
cation.
When the whole truth comes to light
the fact may be presented clearly to
the public of California that Hunting
ton and Herrin had agreed to support
Burns at the time that the corporation
promised to support If. H. de Young.
Therefore, the declaration of Burns to
his followers that he did not become a
candidate until he was goaded to the
extreme by the newspapers was an
afterthought to justify his candidacy.
Whatever may have induced him to be
come a candidate, the stubborn truth
remains that he was not a candidate

when the people -were voting for mem
bers of the Legislature, and he has no
right now to demand a caucus.
Apart from all these considerations
he is not the man from a moral or in
tellectual point of view to represent
California in the Senate. Fifty-eight
of the eighty-five Republicans of the
Legislature are unwilling that he shall
be chosen- .It is possible that these Re
publkans, who are mindful of the honor
and welfare of the State, may lay aside
personal preferences and cast their
united strength in favor of some able
and clean exponent of Republican prin-
ciples.
It is acknowledged that U. S. Grant
has rendered the State ut California
distinguished service in holding his
supporters in line to defeat the designs
of Herrin and Huntington to force
Burns in the Legislature. Before the
session adjourns he may see his way
clear to advise his supporters to render
the people and party another great
service by joining a movement to se
cure the election of a Senator whose
choice will enlist the commendation of
the people.
The lines of the present struggle are
now clearly denned. On one side the
people are ranged and on the other the
agents of the railroad are massed. The
railroad forces are concentrated on one
side. Burns has the supportâ the open
and scandalous support Of the corpora
tion. The representatives of the peo
ple are divided, giving their support to
three candidates, and are daily coming
closer together and before adjourn
ment may consolidate the whole
strength of the people in the Legisla
ture on one man for Senator. The de
feat of Burns and the election of an
acceptable and reputable man to the
Senate would teach the corporation a
lesson that it would not soon forget.
At a late hour to-night there are no
signs of material changes in the Sena
torial situation to-morrow, although
Wednesday of this week was the tithe
designated by Grove L. Johnson for
the election of D. M. Burns.
Irving >I. Scott did not arrive from
San Francisco to-night as expected.
There have been rumors this evening
that Jlr. Scott would gain two votes
to-morrow, but the reports cannot bo
traced to a definite source.
There is a deal of comment among:
I the members of the Legislature over tht>
: utter failure of the touts to influence
legislators. The pet measures of sev
! eral pronounced anti-Burns men have
' passed both houses and received the
; approval of the Governor. Early in the
session the hint was hourly thrown out
that a member who should have the
temerity to oppose the election of Burns
would stand but little chance of secur
i ing the enactment of any cherished
measure of legislation.
Members go down the line and point
to the success of Senators Stratton.
j Davis and Smith in getting their fa
| vored bills through both houses. It is
i clearly evident now to all the leelsla
! tors that The Call was right In assert
ing at the beginning of the session
that the boast of the touts that they
coold control legislation was nothing
more than a bluff.
A public knowledge of these facts has
materially weakened the Burns candi
dacy.
Senator Currier, who went to Los An
geles two or three weeks ago for the
benefit of his health, did not return to
day as his friends hoped, but it is ex
pected that he will arrive to-morrow.
The special election in the Eiehth
Senatorial District to fill the vacancy
caused by the death of Senator John
Boggs will take place to-morrow and
a Republican victory is predicted.

WORKING FOR THE
COURTS OF APPEAL
CALL, HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, March 7.â John A. Wright and
Dr. Taylor, representing the Bar Asso
ciation of San Francisco, appeared -before
the Senate Judiciary Committee this af
ternoon to confer with that body in regard
to Senate constitutional amendment 1,
which relates to the establishment of
courts of appeal. The act is now in a
fair way to proceed on its routine journey,
though nearly two months have been con
sumed in getting it into proper Bbape.
The amendments to it have been numer-
ous and varied. Senators Dickinson,
Smith and Taylor have all had a hand mi
changing the original bill in some way,
while the bar associations of San Fran
cisco and Los Angeles have offered sug
gestions which have borne fruit. The
committee will meet to-morrow and next
day to prepare the bill, and its advent
upon the floor of the Senate will probably
be Friday.

ASSEMBLY RUSHES
APPROPRIATION BILL
CALL. HEADQUARTERS. SACRA
MENTO, March 7.â The Assembly finished
the second reading of the general appro
priation bill to-day and sent It to the
State Printer with instructions to print
it out of order and get it back with no
more than absolutely necessary delay.
Its third reading and passa-9 was made
the special order for to-morrow afternoon
immediately after the Senate special file
is disposed of.
Caminettl of Amador got an amendment
on the agricultural district appropriations
that strikes a hard blow at the county
fair industry by providing that the sums
allowed shall be drawable providing that
no games of chance or gambling games of
any description shall be carried on within
the inclosures. Caminettfs amendment
will work the greatest hardship upon the
Annual State Fair, that of late years has
become little more than good grafting
ground for Joe Harvey and other wheel
of-fortune artists who have made for
tunes here in the years past.
By the amendments that have been add
ed to it during its course through second
reading the total of the appropriations
carried by the bill has been raised $25,600,
but an offset to this is $3000 stricken out
with the position of Commissioner of
Public Works. *
As the bill was introduced in the As
sembly by the Ways and Means Commit
tee it carried a total of $5,097,704 66 : As
it will go to the Senate it foots up Ju.liO.
--304 68 As the bill of two years ago went
to the Governor it totaled $5,343,000.
The amendments that have passed carry
a raise of $14,000 in the postage, mailing,
etc.. allowance of the clerk of the Su
preme Court, a raise of $1000 in the trav
eling expense account of the Governor s
office- $1200 for the Secretary of State
with which to print the constitutional
amendments, $1200 for the traveling and
contingent expenses of the Insurance
Commissioner and a raise of SloOO in his
printing account, $4000 additional to the
printing account of the Bank Commis
sion, and $3500 for the printing account |
of the Harbor Commission. The Stockton
asylum got $6000 additional to its support
fund and $2400 was added to the bill for
the rent of Hastings L.aw College.
This afternoon Grove Johnson suc
ceeded in getting in a further amend
ment carrying $3000 to be used in improv
ing the basement of the Capitol. The im
provement will consist of a restaurant and i
bar room, and will make the "well" with
its villainous whisky a permanent fix
ture of the building.
The umbilical cord that once existed
twixt Dibble and Grove Johnson, and the ,
severance of which Grove announced but '
a few short days ago, was knotted to
gether at the loose ends to-night on the.
same proposition that caused the original
The row occurred over an additional
clerkship in the adjutant general's office,
which the bill created by making an ap
propriation'of $3200 for salary.
It was a pet scheme of Dibble, who is
a strong backer of everything that has to
do With tho G. A. K. or anything arising
out of the late Rebellion. Dibble wanteS
a clerk in the general's office to assume
a Â«hare of the duties, which have multi
plied since the Spanish embroglio and l to
index and catalogue the relics of the Re
bellion and the late unpleasantness Ha
has a bill now in course of legislation
which practically creates the position, and
he was naturally in favor of the appro
priation providing the salary. He was
backed by Valentine. â
Johnson objected, and after a hard ngnt
succeeded in having that clause ex
punged from the bill. To-night it was
broug-ht up again. Johnson gave smÂ«ljnÂ§;
consent, and the action of the other day

THE "BOYS" WILL NOT
HAVE TO GO DRY
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO. March 7.â Although something
of a. temperance body, the Senate has do
dtbiit to stand in the way of any little
refreshment a candidate may be willing
to deal out during a hot campiign. and
with that end in view the Senators hit
with their ax the bill introduced in the
Assembly by Caminetti which aimed a
blow at the ancient and honorable cus
tom of treating the boys. Â....â ,
Shortridge was the one who felt the worst
about it. He said there were some towns
in this State where a candidate could not
get a vote unless he went into every sa
loon in the place. "The bill simply puts
a premium on lying." he declared. "A
candidata will not tell the truth on a sub
ject such as this, and I wouldn t blame
him if he did not. I won't respect that
bill if it is passed. I'll do as I please with
mv money. I'll spend it in a saVoon or
wherever else I please. I'm 21 and I can
spend my own money without asking the
advice of this Legislature."
Wolfe described the bill as idiotic and a
measure that should never pass. It hit
Braunhart in a tender spot, too. for he de
clared the hill would injure one of the
principal industries of his district, for it
robbed him of the statesman's privilege
ot buying steams, sharp and cool, for his
constituents.
The Senate thought that was too much
and snowed the bill under a shower of
noes, among which there was not even
the suggestion of a notice to reconsider.

DOG EAT DOG CLEAR THROUGH
Senators Show the Falsity of the Boast of the
Candelarian Candidate That He Controls Legis
lation and Owns the Legislators.
CALL HEADQUARTERS. SACRA
MENTO. March 7.â Dan Burns and his
gambling partners are not to have the
exclusive privilege of robbing the pen
pie, of the metropolis through the me
dium of the racing game at Ingleside
land Oakland racetracks. This much
â was decided to-day in the Senate by !
a vote that not only killed the poolselling
bills, but also demonstrated the idleness
of the Mexican's boast that he controls
the majority in the Legislature and can
kill any bill that displeases him or pass
any measure that he favors.
This was a field day for gamblers.
Bnd the war that was waged for and
against t;. s of the bills affect
ing their business was as bitter a one
es h; n here for many a day.
It was dog eat dog from start to finish,
end when the smoke o-f combat had
cleared away there was not enough left
cf Dan Burns, Ed Corrigan and their
packers to hold an inquest on.
To-night the poolsellers who came !
here yesterday with the determination i
not to allow Burns and Corrigan to se- |
cure for themselves an exclusive privi
lege for fleecing the public are celebrat- '
ing their victory. As their victory was j
Burns" defeat the .Mexican finds his
dream of a gambling trust, the profits :
of which would have been shared by j
him and his immediate associates in the
racing gums at Ingleside. so badly
shattered that there is no hope of its
future fulfillment.
There were two bills before the Sen
ate, one by Senator Sammy Braunhart
to limit racing to sixty days and the
other by Assemblyman Atherton to
prohibit the selling of pools on any j
sporting event except within the limits j
of a racetrack inclosure. This last bill (
was the most objectionable to the pool- (
sellers, as it was so worded that, had i
It become a law, the sporting resorts ;
where pools are now sold on prize- . j
fights and other sporting events would
have to shut up shop. In other words,
the racetracks were given the exclusive
privilege of fleecing- the public their
trade in that line being legalized by â 
legislative enactment.
- Overtures were made to Burns by the
poolsellers to have the bill killed, the j
argument being advanced that they j
had supported him in his political fights s
and he should now return the favor by \
assisting them. They had, they said, j
done all that lay in their power to elect i
him Senator and it was an ungenerous j
act for him to seek to monopolize the j
gambling industry to their great detri- .
ment. To these pleas Burns turned a
deaf ear, and then it was, and not un- I
til then, that the poolsellers pooled |
their issues and declared war on the j
Mexican.
Burns issued orders to his followers
In the Legislature but not all of them
â would obey him. Some of them were
under more obligations, politically, to j
the pool room men than they are to
Burns, and they elected to vote again:.- â 
the Mexican. Other legislators who
have been voting for other senatorial
candidates than Burns saw in the oc
casion an opportunity to disprove
Burns' assertions that he controls the |
Legislature, so they sided with the pool !
sellers. These legislators said that if |
the law as it stands on the statute
books to-day is not what it should be
it certainly would not better conditions
to pass other laws giving to th few
the privileges that are now possessed
by the gambling fraternity in general.
So it was that whe-n the bills came up
for action in the Senate to-day Burns
: found himself opposed by a formidable
array of votes. Still he refused to sur
render and in desperation called to his j
eid all of his henchmen who are still j
loyal to him. Among these was his
faithful servant, Assemblyman Wright
of Alameda. Wright left his Beat in the
Assembly to lobby for the pool bills on â 
the floor of the Senate, but he is dis- â ,
graced and discredited and his support
of the bills worked to their disadvant- !
age rather than to their good. It was
riot until the pool room men threatened
â Â£, support an amendment that would :
prohibit pool selling on every foot of i
California soil that Burns finally dis- |

The proceedings of the Senate com
menced with the consideration of
Braunhart's substitute for his original
bill 419. Senator Davis was on the
floor at once and submitted an amend
ment, which had the effect of removing
that clause which exempted race I
tracks from the prohibition against |
1 l selling. It made the bill atuilv
everywhere and struck poolselllng

for those poulsellers who did business

At this point, however, Braunhart

and permission was grunted.

one had on the substitute. Ashe
asked if he wanted to kill racing alto
gether, and then Jones wanted to know
why there was racing,' anyway; if it
was not to test the speed of horses what
was it for? To bet? To tempt young
men and women? He believed the
amendment should be passed.
Braunhart said he had introduced

to have some restriction placed on the
vice of poolselling he was willing to

which were offered; he was therefore

did not want it, and ar't^r Wolfe had

A SMOOTH JOB
OF JOHNSON'S
EXPOSED
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, March 7.â Assemblyman Rad
cliff of Santa Cruz stirred up a well-
Btocked hornet's nest in the Assembly
this morning when he called up a resolu
tion of Grove Johnson's, Introduced on
January 14. Mr. Johnson's resolution was
a peremptory order to the Committee on
CommlsHior.s and Public Expenditures to
investigate every commission now fat
tening at the expense of the State and
report to the Assembly which of th<-m
might as well be- dispensed with. Mr.
Johnson's resolution was adopted unan
imously, and that is as far as the mat
tor has gone. The committee has intro
duced no bills, made no recommendations
or any report. The resolution has sim
ply had the effect of staving off legisla
tion against commissions, with the hope
and trust that the committee would do
its duty.
Radcliff, who already has introduced
bills obliterating the Bureau of Highways
and Board of Railroad Commissioners,
saw through the lob yesterday, and this
morning offered the following resolution,
which was adopted:
Whereas, in the early part of this session of
the Assembly a resolution was Introduced by
the Hon. Grove L. Johnson and referred to the
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, MABCH 8, 1899.
Committee on Commissions and Public Ex
penditures to report to this body what public
commissione could be abolished^ in accordance
with the views expressed in Governor Gaffe's
inaugural address; and whereas, this commit
tee has failed to so act up to date. Therefore
be it
Resolved. That the Committee on Commis
sions *nd Public Expenditures be instructed to
forthwith make a report in accordance to the
demands made by said resolution.
Conrey of Los Angeles, chairman of
the derelict committee, replied to Rad
cliff's resolution with the statement that
the committee had done nothing: in the
line of its instructions for the reason that
there is a special commission now in the
pay of the State to fulfill the same du
ties. The committee, he said, would re
port, but its report would amount to
nothing, as it had done nothing. Mr.
Conrey considered that his little state
ment settled the matter, but Melick and
Radcliff did not concur with him.
When the standing committees of the
Assembly were being formed Melick of
Pasadena, who came up here pledged to
fight against unnecessary and expensive
commissions, applied for the chairman
ship of the Commissions Committee. He
was turned down by ex-Speaker Wright.
Radcliff. who came up here pledged the
same way, applied for membership on the
committee. He was turned down in the
same positive manner by the same
Wright.
Then Wright selected his committee
with Gonrey at its head, and juet how
well the committee has abided by the
spirit of its appointment is demonstrated
in the fact that it has brought in no re
port. It has simply pursued the "lay
low" policy and permitted Grove John
son's resolution to have the desired ef
fect, the forestalling of any legislation
that would tend to unload a lot of well
fed and overpaid Job-chasers on the
streets. , .
Radcliff does not propose to stop at his
resolution. He has a bill in course of con
struction which is designed to do the
work the committee was instructed to do,
and unless a satisfactory report Is ren
dered he will introduce It out of order.
DIBBLE JOCKEYS
WITH PLACERVILLE
"CINCH" BILL
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, March 7.â Except for the state
ment that it "was all a mistake" Assem
blyman Dibble is finding it hard to ex
plain the latest jockeying that has been
attempted with Assembly bill 839. Assem
bly bill 839 Is the measure Introduced In
the House by Dibble, as agent for Drury
Melone, the Napa capitalist, and others
who are attempting to cinch the city of
Placerville to the tune of $2+0.000 by legis
lative enactment.
Dibble's first peculiar work was done
when the bill was in the hands of the Ju
diciary Committee. The friends of the
mining town went to him, explained the
crooked intent of the measure, and he
promised to let them know when it was
to come up for consideration. Instead of
keeping his word he got his bill through
one evening with recommendation that it
do pass, and the first notice its opponentb
had of it was when it turned up with
that recommendation on the special file.
Valentine of Los Angeles and Raw of
Placerville demanded that he return it
to Its proper place on the second read
ing tile and then recommit to the Judi
ciary Committee, and Dibble made warm
promises to do as directed.
ili.s second promise proved of as much
value as his first, for, taking advantage
one day of the absence of Valentine and
Raw lie stated from the chair, to which
he had been called, that the opponents of
t.he bill were willing it should go to third
reading and tinal passage and upon his
statement the House passed it along.
Valentine openly accused him of bad
faith next nay, and Dibble, seeing he was
caught, finally had the bill sent back to
commit tee.
The opposition spoke against it one
evening 1 . Drury Melone. whose interests
Dibble is favoring, explained his side of
the proposition on the next. Of the two
stories the committee chose that of the
people of Placerville, and the majority
amended that the bill do not pass. A
minority report was turned in with the
recommendation that the bill do pass,
and between the two reports opportunity
has beer, made for further manipulation
to the advantage of the instigators of the
measure. With a bunch of others the
Judiciary Committee's report was ren
dered yesterday and read from the desk
in the usual way. Then it was sent over
to tho printing office and to-day the As
sembly history came back with the state
ment that the majority had recommended
the bill to pass. The misstatement in the
history was at first laid to inadvertence
on the part of the printer by the oppo
nents of the bill, but further investiga
tion developed that the journal of yester
day carried the same mistake.
Mr. Raw immediately demanded an ex
planation of Dibble, but with a wave of
hand the agent for Melone dismissed fur
ther interrogation with the simple state
ment that it was a mistake. Mr. John
son, chairman of the judiciary committee,
and the other end of the umbilical cord
that binds him to Dibble, also waved hi.s
hand and said "mistake." Then the op
ponents of the bill went upstairs and
dragged over the minutes of yesterday
to see if there had been any interlinea
tions. There were nonej the report showed
plainly that the recommendation of the
majority was that the bill should not
pass.
Then the matter was called to the at- |
tention f>f the Speaker by a motion to
correct both journal and history. The
motion vas carried, and tb,e Investiga
tion continued, but nothing was develop, ci
to Â«how who had done the tine work at
the printing office. Later Dibble smiled :
again and conlided to a representative of !
The <:ill that it was all a mistake, and he '
was glad it was corrected, and Mr. John- j
son did the same thing, hut the Placer j
ville people are not satisfied with the ex
planation and propose to look further In- j
to the matter before allowing it to drop.
Dibble got a chance to get back at As- '
semblyman Raw during the session this
evening. That Is, the chance did not '
come to himâ he created it when it came !
time to take up the Ways and Mean?
unfinished business file. The hour set by
one of Dibble's own peculiar rules for the
consideration of Ways and Means busi
ness Is from 7 to 8:30 o'clock each evening.
Raw had a bill on file and due for its â 
third reading and final passage this even
ing that is of the utmost importance to Xl
Dorado County. It carries the appropria
tion for the J,nke Tahoe road, without
v.hieh that magnificent route to the lake '
cannot be kept open. Raw's bill wa.s due
to pass, and he wanted what was coming
to him. Dibble went down and talked
to Grove Johnson, and when the hour for
the file came along Johnson moved to take
up Senate messages instead. Dibble I
backed him and Raw objected, although :
h< was willing to let it go if Dibble and
Johnson would agree to give the Ways :
and Means file its full time when they j
should come to it. They would not agree,
and kept up thÂ«ir demand for the Senate!
messages, and it was granted them, de-
Bpite that Raw branded their action as
an unfair trick and accused Dibble of
breaking his own rules. When the Ways
and Menus file was finally taken up a
scant three-quarters of an hour was given
to it and Raw's bill went into the- discard.
SENATOR CURRIER
' NEVER FOR BURNS
LOS ANGELES. March 7.â Senator A.
T. Currier, who has partially recovered
from his recent Jllness, snent two hours
in Los Angeles to-day on business. He
left for his home in Spadra early in the
afternoon. The Senator will leave for
Sacramento either on Wednesday or
Thursday night.
Senator Currier, according to his
friends, said but little regarding politics
or the Senatorial embrogllo. Still suffi
cient has been learned from his intimates
to state with positlveness that under no
circumstances would he vote for Burns,
and according to one who Is very close to
Currier, "there Is not enough money in
the kingdom to purchase the Senator's
vote." Although Bulla has been re
moved as a factor. Currier is known to
still desire a Senator from the South, but
whether he will throw his strength to
Grant is not established. Currier has
been heard to say that he admired Gen
eral Barnes very much, which gives that
eloquent gemtleman's friends here en
couragement that the Spadra Senator
may support their choice.

CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO. March 7.â The Assembly Com
mittee on Commerce and Navigation met
this morning and reported favorably upon
bills No. 879 and 880. The bills make It
compulsory for crews of ferry and river
boats to have a regular fire drill. It also
compels the steamship companies to fur
nish complete flre apparatus for their
vessels.

Supervisors commenced an investigation
last night which has for its object the de

i nished the city during the months of De
cember, January and February.

much it costs the company to produce gas
at the present day the Mayor thought it

Edward C. Jones, the company's engi

W. R. Summerhayes of the Mutual Elec

examined. He informed the committee
that in his judgment $3 per night was
ample compensation for the company to
receive for the arc lights furnished the
city.
In answer to questions put to him by
Supervisor Perrault. Mr. Summerhayes
stated that the Mutual Electric Light
Company was prepared to furnish the
city with lights at a much lower figure
than it was paying the San Francisco
Gas and Electric Light Company.
HEAVYWEIGHT SLOGGERS.
The San Francisco Athletic Club held
its monthly fistic entertainment last even
ing on Sixth street ar.d a large crowd was
presentTO see the punchers punch.
Fred Jarrett. the heavy-weight pupil of
Professor Bean, proved to be a formidable
"dub" when he faced James Jeffords the
Cornish miner. Jarrett was <iulte clever
in foot work, but his hitting powers were
far from being dangerous. In fact. Jar
rett has much to learn in so far as
straight hitting and stopping are con-
Jeffords soon discovered that Jarrett
was a novice at the game and. finding In
a mix-up that several excellent openings
were offered for a knock-out, he quickly
availed himself of the opportunity to put
his apponent in his little bed. and with a
few well directed punches Jarrett went to
the hard and cold floor, where he re
mained for eight seconds.
On regaining his pins Jeffords adminis
tered to him a brace of Cornish left-hand
ere which settled all further dispute as
to locel championship. Jarrett took a
tumble and held the floor well in hand un
til counted out.

member of the Board of Visitors to the

olis President McKinley has given the Pa
cific Coast a recognition that is rare in
the history of the naval school and its
annual inspection. The appointment of a
California!) as one of the ten inspectors
cf this institution may be taken as a natu
ral recognition of the new importance
which the Pacific region is to cut in the
naval affairs of the nation.
This Hoard of Visitors is a' creature of
law In usage it is composed mainly of
members of Congress, with one or two
civilians added. Mr. Symmes is this year
one of these civilians. The board is sup
posed to thoroughly investigate the naval
academy and its affairs and to submit to
Congress a report with any recommenda
tions which it may see fit to make

tution and he served three years in the
navy before he retired to the more quiet
paths of commerce. He was a member of
the first class appointed with a view to
creating naval engineers. He organized

THE BRUTUS IN PORT.
The United States collier Brutus reached
port last night from Manila via Guam and
Honolulu. She anchored in the stream
and proceed in a few days to Mare
Island to be overhauled.
The Sahara desert is three times as
large as the Mediterranean.

REARS AN ACADEMY IN
MEMORY OF HER CHILD
NOTRE DAME ACADEMY AT WATSONVILLE.
WATSONVILLE, March 7.â A
large concourse of people from
all parts of the surrounding
country assembled to-day to
witness the laying of the cor
nerstone of the Moreland Notre Dame
Academy. The building will be erected
through the generosity and munificence
of Mrs. Margaret S. Moreland in re
membrance of a daughter - ho died in
the flower of her youth. It is the wish
of the benefactress to bestow upon
girls the blessing of a religious educa
tion. Mrs. Moreland's generosity has
been bestowed without ostentation. Of
a modest and retiring disposition, she
Is of those who "do good by stealth and
blush to call it fame."
Previous to the laying of the corner-
Etone a dinner was served at the Man
sion House upon the arrival of the 1
o'clock train from Pan Francisco.
Plates were laid for 110 guests. Among
the visitors were: Right Rev. Bishop
Montgomery of .Los Angeles, J. Purcell
of the Hibernla Bank of San Fran
cisco, Father Riordan of Menlo Park.
Fathers Murphy and McNamee of
Stti.ta. Cruz, Fathers Farley and- Smith
of Hollister, Father Hudson of Gilroy,
Father Culllgan of Santa Clara Col
lege, Father Mestre of Monterey,
Father Mahoney of Castroville. Father
Ferrar of Gonzales, Hon. J. W. Linn
cott and Hon. Ed Martin of Santa
Cruz.
After the dinnner a large congrega
ton filled the Catholic church and lis
tened to an address by Bishop Mont
gomery. The prelate was at hfs best
and delivered an eloquent discourse on
the necessity of religious principles be
ing Instilled into the minds and hearts
of every one. He especially spoke of
deep religious sentiments being neces
sary in public life. Without these re
straints, he paid, the republic was al
ways in danger. The Bishop spoke
plainly of the combinations and trusts
now being formed all over the land,
forcing the poor people to the wall.
They were seemingly above the law, he
said, and it was very necessary that
some restraining power be brought to
bear upon these oppressors of the lowly.
The Bishop said that In this republic
secular schools were a necessity and
the Catholic church was not opposed
to them, but he claimed the right and
privilege of the Catholic church to build
itÂ« own schools with its own money for
the purposes of religious education.
Thf Bishop's address throughout was
broad and patriotic. He concluded with
a feeling tribute to Mrs. Moreland, the
founder of the structure for which the
cornerstone was to be placed, and
hoped the academy would be a blessir.g
to the people of Watsonville and vÂ£ the
State at all times.
WATERS OF THE
OHIO SUBSIDING
End of the Big Flood in
Sight.
Special Dispatch to Tlie Call.
CINCINNATI, March 7â The end of
the big Hood here is In sight, thanks to
the cold weather prevailing throughout
th" Ohio Valley. At 9 o'clock to-night
the stage here was 56 f>et 8 inches and
rising at the rate of an inch and a
quarter hourly. At all points above
Marietta to-night the river is falling
and it is thought the flood hero will not
go above 57V2 feet.
At Portsmouth the Cincinnati, Ports
mouth and Virginia Railway trains
have been stopped by the floods on the
track and washouts. The suffering by
people driven from their homes In that
city continues.
Huntington, W. Va., reports great
suffering on the Guan River by people
living in the lowlands, who- were driven
from their homes. A steamboat laden
with provisions left Huntiugton for the
relief of these people to-day. Central
City, a small place above Huntington,
is a great sufferer from the flood.
At Newport, Ky., opposite here, fifty
families have been driven from th^ir
homes and have taken refuge in public
buildings or with their friends, and
should the river rise to 57% feet twenty
five more families will be driven out of
their homes. No business houses In
Newport have been affected. In Cin
cinnati all business houses affected by
the flooding of cellars have been amply
protected from any probabe future dan
ger. Dwellings, except the k>w tene
ments in Rat Row and Sausage Row,
have not bepn touched.
ThÂ°re is practically no interruption to
traffic of any sort in Cincinnati. No
trains, however, can now enter the
Grand Central Depot*
th:; iope is well.

Hatteras to Norfolk a terrible storm Is
raging, exceeding In intensity that of

panied by snow and cold. A big vessel,
whose name cannot be ascertained, has
gone ashore at Culls Island near Hat
teras. The life savers are on the scene,
but they fear the storm will prevent
them from rendering any assistance.
The cruiser Brookly. now on her way
to Hampton Roads from Havana to
take on Admiral Schley for Plymouth,
â England, Is thought to be somewhere
off the coast In the storm. She was due
in the roads this morning, but has not
i yet been' reported.

and fears are expressed that many ves
sels have been caught off the Vlrslnia

HONDURAS WILL BE
WADE TO PAY INDEMNITY
NEW YORK, March 7. -A Washington
special to the Herald nays: As a result
of a report made by Commander Logan,
commanding the gunboat Machlas of his
investigation of the murder of ' Frank
Pears, agent of the Pittsbtirg Lumber
Company, at San Juan del Sur, Hondu
| raS the Government of Honduras will
I probably be compelled to punish the mur
derer and pay an indemnity to the rela
tives of the dead man. Before taking
action the State Department will uwait
the report of Consul Allen, who also made
an investigation.
The report of Commander Logan shows
that on February 2 Mr. Pears, who had
been In Honduras three weeks, left his
office to go to his horn* 1 , a short distance
away. The city was thon under martial
law. According to the testimony of Hon
duran soldiers, a sentry hailed Mr. Pears
three times, and then, in accordance with
instructions requiring him to shoot any
body who came within fifty yards and
failed to halt, shot him. The soldier
was court-martialed, but acquitted of the
i charges against him. He immediately
' went Into the Interior. Commander Lo

IN THE INTEREST
OF HERRINGTON
Santa Clara County Ballots
Tampered With During a
Recount.
SAN JOSE. March 7.â There was a
sensational turn to the Herrington-
CampbeM contest over the District At
torneyghlp to-day when Judge Hyland
announced he had discovered signs of
fraud perpetrated during the recount.
Not only this, but the guilty persons
had left their identification in the shape
of thumb marks and a dermatologist of
the Pudd'nhead Wilson type would
have no trouble In Identifying them.
All the ballots tampered with were in
favor of ex-District Attorney B. A.
Herrington, the contestant. On these
a cross different from those used on
election day had been employed, and
this Is what led to the discovery of this
most palpable fraud. In all probability
the stamp had been glued to some one's
thumb and inked with a pad in the
pocket.
Word was Bent to the attdrneyÃ on
both sides to be present In court this
morning, and It was supposed Judge
Hyland was ready to hand down a de
cision in the contest. Instead he an
nounced the discovery of fraud and
announced the reopening of the case to
allow the contestant's side a chance to
clear Itself of suspicion. The court
room was crowded and the announce
ment came as a thunderclap from a
clear sky.
Judge Hyland said he deemed it ad
visable to call the attorneys together
to make a few suggestions to them and
also to aid him In detecting the guilty
persons. In the election contests for
Sheriff's office. City Justice and County
Treasurer everything had gone along
smoothly. In the Herrington-Camp
bell contest it was different. attorneys
had flocked about Herrington to the
number of seven while Campbell and
only two others had looked after his
interests. Ballots were first passed to
attorneys for contestant to be exam
fned and each of these *^â¢ ***
handled the ballots. On February 6 the
Judge had been compelled to limit the
number of attorneys on both side* to
three. This left Attorneys Ho*ell
Moore F. C. Jacobs and B. A. Her
rington for contestant. hen the bal
lots were all counted the contestant
rested. Campbell then objected to the
Ninth Precinct being counted because
of irregularities. He also offered testi
mony to impeach six ballots . from , i
land Precinct. On four of these ballots
there were crosses which it was alleged

WHY THE KAISER
SENT CONDOLENCE
Making Capital of Kip
ling's Bereavement.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
BERLIN, March 7.â Emperor Wil
liam's messagre of sympathy to Mrs.
Rudyard Kipling, occasioned by her
husband's serious Illness, is commented
upon by the press here to-day. The
Vosslehe Zeitung says:
"This- new manifestation of the Em
peror's, and the recent one by German
Ministers, form a pleasant contrast to
the erroneous statements that Germany
Is about to declare a tariff war on the
United States. The German nation, on
the contrary. Is strongly desirous of
living in peace with America, economi
cally as well; but hopes for full reci
procity and that the legitimate inter
ests of Germany will be considered."
These remarks and Emperor "Wil
liam's action are apparently based on
the theory of his Majesty that Rudyard
Kipling's residence is in the United
States; that the fact that his wife is
an American and his sympathy for this
country make him an American in
spirit, if not by birth, as the distin
guished author was born In Bombay
December 30, IS6O.
The Post says: "The great cordiality
with which our monarch honors Mr.
Kipling ought to be calculated to re
mind Americans,- after a period of arti
ficial estrangement which we trust is
past, that they will nowhere find read
ier and more joyful recognition of the
brilliant features of their character
than from their cousins here and firstly
from our imperial leader."
NEW YORK, March 7.â Mr. Double
day to-night reported both Rudyard
Kipling and his little daughter Elsie
to be slowly but surely convalescing.
Mr. Kipling has not yet been informed
of the death of Josephine and he will
not be told about it until he is prac
tically well. She was his favorite and
the physicians dread the possibility of
a relapse should he learn of her death.
Some of the more important cablegrams
and messages are daily read to Mr.
Kipling, and occasionally he is allowed
to read them himself. Every message
is carefully censored, however, to pre
vent the father reading even an allu
sion to his loss. Great quantities of
flowers continue to be sent to the Gre
noble Hotel by friends of the Kiplings.
At 10 o'clock to-night Mr. Double
day said Mr. Kipling had done splen
didly during the day and had made
more progress toward recovery than on
any preceding day.
Mr. Doubleday, In deference to the
wishes of Mrs. Kipling, refused to give
any information regarding the ar
rangements for the funeral of Jospph
ine Kipling. The child, Mrs. Kipling
says, in no sense belonged to the pub
lic, and she wishes to avoid havine the
funeral take on the character of a
spectacle.
Rudyard Kiplinfr. it is reported, will
be elevated to the peerage on January
1. 1900. Dr. Neal McPhatter of Edin
burgh. Scotland, who is staying at the
Windsor Hotel, says he has received
the news from Sir Walter Besant. He
adds that the report is common gossip
in the literary circles of London.
Mrs. Kipling, accompanied by her
sister, Mrs. Dr. Dunham, and Dr. Con
land of Brattleboro, Vt.. early to-day
left the hotel where the author lies.
Shortly after 2 o'clock they returned
with fio- ers in their hands. It was
presumed that they had been to the
funeral of Mr. Kipling's daughter
Josephine.

Rumors That Cause the Return of the
Detroit to Blueflelds.
COLON, Colombia. March T. - The
United States gunboat Marietta arrived
here from Blueflelde late yesterday, bring
ing news that all waa quiet there up to
four days ago. There were rumors of fur
ther troubles in the interior. The United
States cruiser Detroit has just received
orders to return to Blueftelds forthwith.
She will leave to-day, although she has
not finished coallnp.
News just received from Bocas del Toro
confirms the reported arrival there of
General Reyes, the head of the recently
collapsed revolution at Blueflelds The
general reached there on March 4 on
board the schooner Buena Ventura
WASHINGTON. March 7. - The De-
Eartment of State has been cabled by Mr
orsby, our Consul at San Juan del None'
as follows:
"Captain Symonds of the Marietta ar
rived from Blueflelds reports Reyes left
March 1 for Colombia; revolution ended
order prevails in Blueflelds. All for
eigners in Bluefields engaged in revolution
previously entitled to protection of con
sulates left with passports March 2 for
New Orleans. Blueflelda commerce unin
terrupted."
The department has been informed by
Mr. Clancy at Bluefields that the Gov
ernment troops took peaceful possession
of Roma yesterday, ending the revolution.

DYEA BUILDINGS
HAZED BY FLAMES
Conflagration Visits the
Alaskan Port.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SEATTLE, March 7.â Dyea had its
first big fire on the evening of March
1, resulting- in the razing of five build
ings and contents and a loss of over
$12,000, with no insurance.
The fire started in the Palace Hotel,
in the extreme southern end of the
town. It originated in the room of the
proprietor's wife, Mrs. McCue, while all
were at dinner. When discovered the
flames had made such headway that
they were beyond control and soon the
entire building was ablaze.
Dyea has no fire apparatus and is de
pendent solely on a bucket brigade.
This was quickly formed, 200 men
standing in a double line to the river
close by. Efforts were made to save
adjoining buildings and a building on
the opposite side of the street. The
fire-fighters failed on the first, but suc
ceeded on the last.
The Northern Hotel, adjoining the
Palace, was soon ablaze and was tot
ally destroyed, and the Chilcoot stable
of the Chilcoot Tramway, adjoining
the Northern to the south, also was de
stroyed. This stable was full of
horses, but these and all the wagons
were saved, and the bare stable and a
small quantity of feed were destroyed.
North of the Palace Hotel, where the
fire originated, the Senate saloon and
the Courthouse fell victims to the
flames. Several buildings on the oppo
site side of the street caught fire, but
were saved, In one hour's time" the
fire had burned itself out. Very little
furniture was saved from any of the
buildings destroyed. _
The glare of the fire in Dyea was
plainly visible In Skaguay. across the
mountains.

Storage rnsts money in Dawson.

his main operations are confined to |
trading in the Dawson market.

PLANS FOR THREE
BIG BATTLESHIPS
Will Equal Best Class
in the World.
Special tMspatch to The Call.
Call Headquarters, Wellington Hotel,
Washington, March 7.
The plans for the three big battle
ships authorized by the naval appro
priation bill just passed are being
worker! out. The general features of
the ships have been defined, subject to
the approval of the Secretary. The
battleships v.-il! by a thousand tons
larger than the Maine and Missouri
class, of a total displacement of 13,500
tons, making them by far the largest
Fhips in the American navy and about
equal in dimensions to the best type of
battleships now being constructed
.'i broad under the new practice. Though
larger than the Maine, the ships will be
laid on almost the same lines, being
r-nly a little greater in every dimension.
They will carry each four 12-inch guns
in turrets. This caliber is an Inch less
than the guns carried by the Oregon,
but by improved design they will be al
most equal in penetrative power.
The fc-inch guns of the Oregon type,
although they did very effective work
at Santiago and Manila have been
abandoned. But instead of eight 8-inch
guns the new ships will have no less
than sixteen guns of 6-inch caliber, all
rapid-firers and twenty 6-pounders.

blank book paper, evidently a leaf torn
from a book. The writing and spelling
wÂ»re very poor. Mr. Cormack, after
recovering fr^in his surprise, immedi
ately notilied Mrs. Houseworth and told

Mr. Ormaok delivered the will to the

for probate before Judge John C. Gray
of Butte County.
Thfn followed another sensation. Mrs.
Houseworth, under oath, declared < 'â  r
mark had illegally retained possession
of the will and demanded $2000 before
he would deliver it. In order to obtain
the precious paper she consented to pay
that sum when she. came into th" es
tate. She also swore that Cormack had
pledged her not to mention his name in
connection with the case, i.'irmacks
reputation for honesty, rectitude and
probity had never before been ques
tioned and every one who know him
was surprised and on all sides the wo
man's stury was discredited.
Under instructions from Judge Gray
the Grand Jury began an investigation
into the alleged illegal detention of
Fullers will and the alleged attempt at
extortion, but nothing has as yet come
of the inquiry.
Mr. Cormack was one of Fuller's
closest friends and the mysterious send
ing of the will to him. months after a
futile search had been made for it, is
considered by the Fuller heirs as a part
of the scheme to deprive them of th^ir
rights. The heirs allege in the contest
that this procedure was simply to add
weight to the forgery. Cormack repu
diates the whole affair and says he had
nothing to do with it further than to
receive the will in the strange manner
detailed and that he immediately de
livered it to Mrs. Houseworth.
It is further alleged by friends of
Alfred Fuller that he did not have any

afterward, but the couple are now liv

This battery represents the ideal pro
portion between guns, taught by the
experience of our own fleet during the
Spanish war and of the Chinese and
Japanese vessels during their late war.
The speed of the ships will be IS knots,
an advance of two knots over the Ore
gon class, but just equal to that of the
n<'w Maine. For protection against the
enemy's shot the battleships will have
but one b^it of armor seven feel six
inches broad, running clear around the
water line, three feet six inches above
and four feet below. It was originally
planned to have this armor sixteen
inches thick, but the designers have
now reduced the thickness to twelve
inches. This change was made in order
to strengthen the casements, which
will be made seven and a half inches
thick instead of five inches

; Slayer of a Merchant Not Wanted in
Everett.
TACOMA. March 7.â lt was reported to
i day that an attempt had been made at
i Everett to lynch Kditor J. W. Connella.
who shot and killed Merchant Olsen at
Everett several months ago. At his sub
sequent trial in Kitsap County on a
change of venue Connella was acquitted.
A mass-meeting then held at Everett
j passed resolutions requesting that i'<m
' nella should not return there. He an
nouced his intention to return when he
got ready, and went East to visit rela
tives. Upon his return, two days ago, he
was waited upon and asked to leave town.
He declined to go. It is reported to-night
from Everett that the feeling against
Connella remains strong there, though no
violence has been attempted.

ISSUE OF
BONDS NOT
NECESSARY
How Expenditures Author
ized by Congress Will
Have to Be Met.
If Required the Government Can
Seen 'c Additional Cash by Sell
ing Central Pacific Notes.
Special Pispafh to The Call'
NEW YORK. March 7.â The Wash
ington correspondent of the Herald tel
egraphs: All expenditures authorized
by f'nngress for this fiscal year and the
next will be m*Â»t without the sale of
bonds or the issue of certificates of in
debtedness.
W'h'--n Sf-rretary Gage Issued his an
nual report in December he estimated
a deficit of J112.000.000 for the current
fiscal year and a little more than $30.
--000,000 for the nf>xt year ending June 30,
19f'O. The estimates made by Repre
sentatives Cannon and Dockery of the
House Committee on Appropriations
put the deficiency for the next year far
ab ive the figures of the Secretaryâ
Dockery placing 't as high as $100,000,
--000.
These figures, it is contended by
Treasury officials, are much too high.
Secretary Gage's estimate of revenue
for next year is $^10.9r.5.112. and the
appropriations for that year amount to
$673,658,400. assuming that the deficiency
appropriations for that year will be as
large as for this, which is a very liberal
estimate. This would make an appar
ent deficit of $52,700,288. It is expected.
however, that actual expenditures will
fail somewhat short of the total appro
priations, for it Is not believed such
large deficiencies^ will have to be made
up because it is expected there will be
a surplus in the appropriation for the
army.
About $10,000,000 will be received from
the Central Pacific Railway, and this
will reduce the deficit to about $100,000,
--000. enabling the treasury to begin the
fiscal year with a sufficient cash bal
ance on hand to relieve all apprehen
sion.
Should it become necessary for the
treasury to secure more cash than is
brought In by ordinary' receipts during
the next few years the Government
may do so by selling Central Pacific
Railway notes which the law authorizes
the Secretary of the Treasury to do for
not less than par and accrued interest.
While there is no probability that
there will be a treasury deficiency after
that time it is apparent that the war
revenue taxes will have to be main
tained indefinitely if Congress con
tinues to appropriate money on the
same scale as in the session just closed.
RUHLIN WINS IN
THE FIFTH ROUND
Easily Defeats Old Joe
Coddard.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, March. 7.â Gus Ruhlin
of Akron, 0., met and defeated Joe
Goddard of Australia in the fifth round
of what was to have been a twenty
round bout before the Lenox Club to
night. Both men stripped in excellent
condition, but Ruhlin had the call in
the betting. This was certainly justi
fied from his appearance, and from the
outset he held his opponent cheaply.
He evidently saw that Joe could not
offset his rushes and from the begin
ning forced the fighting. The Aus
tralian went down frequently with good
cause, but toward the close of the con
test he dropped without cause and was
deservedly disqualified. Ruhlin was a
2 to 1 favorite in the betting.
Round IâGoddard1 â Goddard opened with a left to
'> the body, landing lightly. Gus landed
1 with left and right on the head and body
: and was cautioned for hitting in the
j breakaway. A light left from Gus sent
! Goddard to the floor, but the old-timer
was up immediately, on!y to go down
again with a right on the jaw. This time
I Goddard stayed down eight seconds.
! When h" "Ot up he feinted with his left
and was met with a hard right on the
fare, and wont down a third time, re
maining on the floor almost to the limit.
When he got up there were only a few
\ seconds left before the round ended.
Round 2â Ruhlin sent his left to the
' face hard and Goddard dropped three
; times in quick succession. Ruhlin re
peated this dose and knocked his man
down with each blow. The referee count
ed six and nine and eight consecutively
while Goddard was trying to regain his
j feet, and the referee cautioned Goddard
â  to stop "holding."
R.iund 3â Joe was quick in toeing the
mark, and jumped to the center of the
ring. He dodged a vicious left from Ruh
-1 lin. but wont to his knees with a hard
right on the jaw. After a lot of fiddling
! Gus forced Joe to the ropes in the oppo
j site corner and put him down with a right
I smash on the jaw. The "old hoss" seemed
; used to such rough treatment and came
i up on the defensive, only to go down
; again with another smash on the jaw.
T ward the end of the round they were
both again cautioned for holding.
Round 4â Ruhlin landed two left hooks
i and a right on the head, which sent God
! dard down. He stayed down eight sec
i onds and came up. only to dodge a vi
i cious left hook for the head, but it landed
!In the shoulder and down he went.
' Throughout the remainder of the round
Goddard remained on the defensive
Round sâ Goddard opened with a left
> lead for the body, but Ruhlin sent a right
j and left in quick succession in the face,
i Then Ruhlin sailed in with a series of
I right and left hand smashes, every one of
! which landed hard, and Goddard accepted
them like an iron man. Each man was
: again cautioned by the referee to avoid
! holding, but Goddard could not withstand
! Ruhlin's onslaughts and dropped to the
1 floor without a blow to escape punlsh
: ment. This being obviously a transgres
! sion of Queenshury rules. Referee White
I ordered the boxers to their corners and
! declared Ruhlin the winner.
WILL PRAY FOR RAIN.
Los Angeles Methodists Designate a
Day for Fasting.
LOS ANGELES, March 7.â The Meth
odist ministers of Los Angeles and
vicinity will seek to learn why rain is
withheld fmm Southern California.
Wednesday of next week has been set
asido as a day of fasting, humiliation
and prayer. The committee that was
appointed by the Methodist Ministers'
Association to draft resolutions on the
situation has reported. The chairman
said the situation in certain districts
WAS appalling. The commitee decided
there was need of rain in the spiritual
as well as In the physical world. No
recommendation was made that the
churches pray for rain, but it was sug
gested they pass a day in humiliation
and fasting, and ank God why he has
sent this drought and what he wishes
his people to da.
