THE END OF AN EVIL.
TIRE is every reason to suppose that racing,
which has degenerated in this community into
a vicious form of gambling, is about to be de
prived of its power for ill. Theie is firm ground for
the hope that the bookmaker, into whose hands daily
there has been passing thousands of dollars, some of
it stolen, much of it representing the earnings of
dupes, will have to seek other fields in which to ply
their vocation. San Francisco has stood the drain too
long, and too long calmly endured the spectacle of
and women being wrecked morally and finan
cially for the benefit of the promoters of "sport," and
an army of vagrants and touts.
The subject has been thoroughly exploited. The
story has been told again and again in the records
. I the courts ami the Morgue. It has been told in a
long list of embezzlements, betrayals of trust, sun
dered families, suicides and murder. Xo defense can
be made of the sort of racing permitted at Ingleside.
It is simply a gamble, with the percentage all in fa
vor of the professional gambler. The owners of
hordes will still be allowed to hold speed contests for
purses, and if they are sincere in their assertion that
they merely desire to encourage the breeding of good
stock, they can have no reason to complain. Indeed,
they might consistently aid the reform, since one
effect of it will be to make their calling respectable.
The majority of the Board of Supervisors is to be
congratulated. The ten members have shown a keen
appreciation of the demand for radical reform, and
they have boldly undertaken a task which will incur
for them the enmity of an element heretofore wont to
believe itself all-powerful.
Ingleside racetrack proprietors threaten if bereft of
their present unholy perquisites to nail up the gate
and go to Oakland. Perhaps this is a bluff; if not,
it is hard luck for Oakland. They will be obliged to
make a threat more dire than this before San Fran
cisco will get scared.
COLLAPSE OF c BOODLE BOOM.
THERE is a point in all speculation beyond which
it is dangerous to go. A steady supply equal to
the demand always tends to maintain prices and
keep the market constantly in a healthy and receptive
condition, whereas the moment speculators attempt to
force prices up or down clouds overspread the hori
zon, squalls break, hurricanes gather and everything
goes to smash. The causes of these commercial phe
nomena are overproduction, oversupply, fictitious
prices or the creation of a market in which nobody
has confidence.
The commercial law here imperfectly described ap
plies to legislative boodling as well as to the ordinary
boodhng of commerce. The maxim deduced might
be stated thus: Even political boodling has a boom
point beyond which it is dangerous to go. The
latest illustration of the operation of the law is found
in Oakland.
The Common Council of that city had a good thing
in the Contra Costa and Oakland water companies.
These corporations have shown on numerous occa
sions that rather than have their rates ''regulated"
under the February provisions of the constitution they
would come down handsomely, or, as it is expressed
in Oakland circles, "put up" gorgeously. Successive
Common Councils have worked the Alameda water
lords out of many an easily earned dollar, and fixing
water rates annually has long been considered a profit
able perquisite by the bosses, political parasites and
blackmailers of the coast Athens.
But according to ail accounts the boodlers this year
have overdone the business; in fact, they have forced
the price of votes for fixing rates beyond the boom
point, and consequently have not only demoralized
the market, but completely destroyed it. The mani
pulators who set out to make the two corporations
"come down" or "put up" â whichever you please â â¢
were on Monday thrown into confusion by the ac
ceptance by the corporations of what is designated in
water circles throughout the State as the "minority
order!" This schedule was framed by Councilman
Upton, and on being put upon its passage wasr
adopted by a vote of 8 to 3 â three Councilmen
friendly to the water companies having been directed
by them to vote for it. Thus the water lords escaped
the clutches of the politicians and demonstrated once
more that even boodling can be overworked.
There is but one conclusion to be drawn from the
facts of this case â the price demanded by the water
rate fixers must have been terrifying. Otherwise the
corporations would never have considered the Upton
ordinance. Were the boodlers misled by the dry
year? Something must have inspired the extraor
dinary confidence which they displayed.
COME TO THE POINT.
IT is impossible to bring the supporters of Dan
Burns to a point, because they have no point
that will bear publicity. An admission on their
part that there is any standard by which a candidate
for the Senate of the United States should be tested
would show a little respect for the public. Then, if
they would concede that the standard ought to com
prise brains, education, knowledge, experience and
integrity, a further step would be gained. If they
climbed thus far out of the mire in which they appear
to be stuck they might probably acknowledge that the
manipulation of unclean politics, State or municipal,
is an unseemly preparation for the consideration and
settlement of great national affairs. By this time
they would have almost reached the level of practical
citizenship of a comparatively decent type, although
they would still fall short of the lofty ideals of Ameri
can statesmanship. There would remain only two
questions to be settledâ first, has Burns any of the
qualifications enumerated? and, second, is not the
conceded disqualification the sole claim that has been
advanced on his behalf?
But, compared to the Burns touts, who have lost
their sensibility and their senses by riding on rail
road tracks, the hide of the hippopotamus is thin,
the hearing of the adder acute and the vision of the
mole sharp. They are equally stolid and impervious.
Their only watchword is "Burns," and the only other
word they know in its full signification is "Boodle."
Party names, still more political principles, for them
have no meaning. The voice of the people may swell
to a roar, but it will not disturb their equanimity.
Patriotism and Americanism are as alien to them as
virtue to a courtesan or the sanctity of an altar to the !
inmate of a dive.
There is no remedy, therefore, but to batter these
political Filipinos back to the brush from which they
emerged, and this unpleasant labor cannot be done
by the press alone. Citizens and their chosen repre
sentatives must finish the job. They at least are not
bent on satiating personal malice by disguising a
railroad saint as a political sinner. They are not
jealous proprietors of obstreperous newspapers that
meanly advocate political decency and the preserva
tion of the honor of the State. Nominally they own
the State, and they really Have its fame and its in
terests in their keeping. They have some right to in
quire who Dan Burns is and what he has ever said
or done, what qualities or associations he has that re
lieve his pretensions from sheer audacity. They may
be permitted to insist that a railroad tool, with the
reputation of a defaulter, with a name at once mentally
blank and morally malodorous, has no vested right or
titie. merely because of these facts, to a Federal Sen- !
atorship. They may even be pardoned for concentrat- j
ing their influence and their votes upon the prompt |
election of a Republican, a statesman and a gentle-
THE COUNTY GOVERNMENT BILL.
ARGUMENTS p r e sente d by the delegation of
citizens from Santa Clara County against the
county government bill are sufficiently strong
to justify its rejection, even if not another county
made a protest or not another word of opposition was
uttered. They were made up of plain statements of
the effects the bill would have upon the government
of Santa Clara County itself, and they have therefore
all the force of stubborn facts not to be gainsaid or
ignored.
The Santa Clara men, several of whom are county
officials, pointed out that the bill if enacted will in
crease the cost of government in their county by up
ward of $50,000 a year without in any way increasing
the effectiveness of the administration of county af
fairs or promoting the general good. The statement
was supported by an analysis of the various ways by
which the cost of government would be augmented.
From the showing thus made it appears the cost of
county government in Santa Clara would be increased
by the salary of a clerk of Board of Supervisors
$125 a month, a Deputy Sheriff $1800 a year, a Li
cense Collector $150 a month; two additional assist
ants in the office of District Attorney, one at $1200
and the other at $1500 a year; a Deputy County Sur
veyor at $1000 a year; and. furthermore, the Sheriff
is to have additional fees and the Surveyor is to have
besides his deputy such other assistance as may be
necessary.
It must be borne in mind that the county has al
ready a Supervisors' clerk, a Deputy Sheriff, a License
Collector, and the District Attorney has two assist
ants in his office. The proposed additions will there
fore add nothing to the service of the county. Their
effect will be merely the creation of new salaries.
There are, moreover, other objections to the bill.
Under it Township Justices are to receive $150 a
month and be furnished with an office and a clerk at
$75 a month; constables are to receive $125 a month
and free office room; Supervisors are to be author
ized to let contracts for bridge building without call
ing for bids, and are to have control of all county
printing.
Such would be some of the effects of the proposed
bill upon counties of the class to which Santa Clara
belongs. It is not strange, therefore, that her press
and her people should protest against it. It might
well be entitled a bill to increase the number of
county officers, the rate of salaries and the burden of
taxation.
The people of Santa Clara deserve credit for the
attention they have given this subject and the vigor
with which they have opposed it. If in every part of
the State the citizens were equally vigilant and atten
tive to public matters California would be much bet
ter governed than it is. After all the people of a free
country generally get the kind of legislation they de
serve, and Santa Clara clearly merits a good govern
ment, for she has shown a willingness to study public
affairs and to work for economy, both at home and at
Sacramento.
WAR CONGRESS APPROPRIATIONS.
\A R. CANNON'S statement of the causes lead
/ \ ing to the enormous appropriations made by
* * the late Congress is in the main sufficiently
satisfactory to offset any objections that may be urged
cither by the partisans of the- opposition or by ex
treme economists. The Republican party can safely
support the work of the war Congress before the peo
ple, and with justice ask popular approval of almost
every item of the vast expenditure, which in the ag
gregate amounted to more than a billion and a half
of dollars.
There were two items of increased expenditure,
however, which the intelligence of the country will
not approve. The first of these is the increase in
pensions over those of last year to the amount of
$4,000,000, and the second is the increase over last
year of $16,000,000 for the postal service. In neither
of these items should there have been any material
increase of expenditure whatever. On the contrary,
the country had a reasonable right to expect a de
crease in each.
The war with Spain has of course added to the
names on the pension roll, and the nation does not
grudge one dollar of expense thus honorably imposed
THE SAST FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, MARCH 8, 1899.
upon the taxpayers. But there should be a decrease
in the former pension rolls. The Civil War closed
thirty-five years ago, and yet the number of pen
sioners claiming pensions for service in that war con
tinues to increase, and the end is not yet. That some
thing of fraud exists in this condition of affairs is
beyond question, and the Republican party, whose
loyalty to the veterans who followed Grant and sup
ported Lincoln in the war for the Union is not
doubted, should undertake the work of purging the
pension lists of dishonest claimants and make it what
it should beâ a roll of honor for the deserving brave.
For the increased expenditure for the Postoftice
there is no justification whatever. The postal ser
vice in Great Britain, in France and in Germany is
self-supporting, and even yields a revenue to the
state. There is no reason why similar results should
not be obtained in this country. Congressman Loud,
chairman of the Postal Committee of the House, has
repeatedly urged a reform in the sen-ice which would
greatly diminish the expenditures and get rid of the
deficit. Every effort in that direction, hoover, ha
failed, and as a result every increase in the business
of the Postoffice is followed by an increase of the de
ficit.
With the exception of the items noted, the appro
priation bills of the war Congress will stand the test
of popular judgment. We have fought a great na
tion and conducted a war on both sides the globe
without pausing in our work of internal improvements
or checking in any way our ordinary expenditures for
the upbuilding of the republic. The burden has been
vast, but upon the mass of the people it sits so lightly
it is hardly noted. Speaker Reed once answered the
attacks of the opposition upon the liberal appropria
tions of the Fifty-first Congress, with the epigram,
"This is a billion dollar country." He can now say it
is growing fast to be a two billion dollar country and
the people stay with it.
SENATOR MORGAN, the sincerity of whose
support of the Nicaragua canal project is not
to be doubted, is reported to be well satisfied
with the results attained by the friends of the mea
sure in the late Congress. A letter attributed to him
says: '"The earnest and patriotic purpose of the
President to construct a canal has been responded to
by Congress in a decisive and conclusive way, and the
foundation has been laid, which will never be shaken,
for this work of universal benefaction."
It is to be hoped this sanguine view of the situation
is correct, for it is much more hopeful than that taken
by the public. Despite the high authority of the Sen
ator, however, there will be widespread doubt of its
correctness. The Senator is growing old and has
acquired a habit of seeing things as he desires them
rather than as they are. It is possible, and even prob
able, that in this case, as in many others, he has per
mitted the wish to be father to the thought, and in
dulge? the belief that he has accomplished much for
the canal, when as a matter of fact the enterprise has
been stalled for years to come.
Congress voted an appropriation for a survey of
all the suggested routes for the canal, but not a dollar
for beginning construction on any of them. Under
the act the commission charged with the survey will
have to report upon the Panama route as well as upon
that of Nicaragua. This on the face of it is a victory
for the Panama Company. Their project now stands
on the same footing with that of Nicaragua, and an
opportunity is afforded them to make a strong fight
when the next Congress meets.
With two projects undjpr discussion it will be easy
for the opponents of the measure to set up one
against the other and thus provoke a rivalry which
will indefinitely postpone action. If public sentiment
becomes divided as to which route would be the bet
ter, there will be an inevitable weakening of the
forces in favor of a canal, while the antagonists of the
enterprise will remain as firmly united as ever, and
have the further advantage of pretending to support
one project or the other, while really preventing the
adoption of either.
We have seen such tactics employed with success
for a good many \ r ears by the opposition to the pro
posed reforms in the postal regulations designed to
make the service self-supporting. Two plans were
advanced in the late Congress for that purpose â one
proposed to check the abuse of the second class mail
privilege, which entails such a heavy expense upon
the service; the other proposed to reduce the
amounts paid to the railroads for carrying the mails.
The adoption of either of these would have resulted
in a saving sufficient to put an end to the annual
deficit in the Postoftice Department.
When the first plan was submitted to the House,
however, it was defeated by men who said they pre
ferred the second plan. When the second was sub
n.itted it was defeated by men' who declared them
selves in favor of the first plan. Thus nearly every
member of Congress made a record in favor of pro
viding a remedy for abuses in the postal service and
establishing the service on an economical basis, and
yet nothing was accomplished. Congress, it appears,
was well nigh unanimous that something should be
done, and equally unanimous in resolving not to do it.
There is a danger that such tactics win now be used
against the canal enterprise. The friends of Panama
will vote against Nicaragua, and the friends of
Nicaragua will oppose Panama. Such is the situation
as it appears to-day, and it behooves the real ad
vocates of the enterprise to be active and vigilant, or
the results in the next Congress will be only another
postponement and an appropriation for another
survey.
One of the Supervisors declared in open session
that he was not acquainted with "Colonel Mazuma,"
which, so far as any personal relations with him are
concerned, might easily be true. But Colonel Mazuma
has a right to feel offended to learn that his identity
is unknown to any citizen of California.
Rev. Dr. Brown explains that he never denounced
Dan Burns, but that, on the contrary, he admires the
gentleman from Mexico. We freely add the informa
tion that the Brown in question is not the one of
Overman fame.
"Smiling" Billy Hamilton now has to do the work
which the Government pays him for doing, and his
smile is only a memory. It was the Hamilton idea
to draw a salary and put in his time whooping things
up for Burns.
There is a familiar aspect about the proposition to
build a hospital for the city and county to take the
place of the rickety thing which now insults the in
digent citizen. Such a rumor is always in order.
California will be glad to congratulate Shafter upon
being made a major-general, but it was a cruel blow
for the Examiner.
Announcement is made that Alger is coming to
this coast. His reception will depend upon whether
he shall come before or after resignation.
Reports that Fitzsimmons has a bad elbow do not
interest the public much, but may be valuable for
poolroom purposes.

A special committee of the Manufactur
ers' and Producers' Association was at
work yesterday upon a matter th;it
in â  ;i nt the pain or loss of millions of
dollars to this city and State. Some time
ago Colonel Long of the quartermaster's

>( :tr or more has been to furnish supplies
and tit out the Government transports
for Uncle Sam, requested of the State
Harbor Commissioners ;i suitable place
fur the docking of vessels under his
charge. He represented that suitable
docking: facilities are absolutely necessary
in order to meet the demands of the Gov
ernment in dispatching the transports
that carry provisions, munitions of war
and soldiers to the Pacific squadron in
Manila as will as the other parts of the
Western ocean.
Colonel Long called particular attention
to the fact that in the past he has fitted
out these expeditions at great inconveni
ence to himself and the department. That
was during the emergencies of the late
war, when it was necessary to make the
of everything.
Recent developments indicate that the
T'nited States will continue to hold many
or possibly all of the new Pacific posses
sions and that tlvre will be a large
amount of shipping by the Government
from this side of the continent to the
Orient. Hence, suitable docking facilities
are an absolute necessity.
The officers of the Manufacturers' and
Producers' Ass.. nation were stirred to
quick action yesterday by the information
that Seattle is making an effort to draw
1 â¢ ilovernment supply department away
from San Francisco and install it in the
Puget Sound city. A short time ago the
Seattle Chamber of Commerce beld a
meeting and discussed the request of Co!
--onel Long made to the California State
officers. The result of that meeting was
telegraphed to the department at Wash
ington and to Colonel Long. It was noth
ing Less than an offer to the Government
of free dockage and the granting of
every concession that the department
would ask if the Pacific base of supplies
would be moved from San Francisco to
Seattle. Attention was called to the fine
harbor of the north and to the railroad
facilities of tin.' northern route over which
munitions and supplies obtained in the
East could be shipped to that port.
The local manufacturers detemined that
the advantage already gained by San
Francisco could not properly be allowed
to be iost. A meeting was therefore held
yesterday and the situation was thor
oughly discussed. The argument was pre
sented that this port is the besi adapted
to the shipping of supplies in the Pacific,
being centrally situated. The department
is already located here, and the benefits
to the State received during the past few
months show that during the late war
supplies of various kin. ls amounting to
about two and a half million dollars were
purchased here, and since then much more
Government money has found its way Into
the pockets of local manufacturers. With
purchases already made and goods sent i
from the East, aside from ammunition, j
the Government has shipped from the
port of San Francisco cargoes valued at
over eight million dollars.

would be commercial suicide, and a com
mittee, consisting of J. W. Kerr and
Manager Goodwin, started out for Colonel
Long with whom a conference was hold,
and later a visit was paid to the Harbor
Commissioners. The result of their efforts,

isfactory, and at the next meeting they
will report that the patronage of the
Government has been saved to this city
through the timely action of the Manufac
turers' Association and the Harbor Com
missioners.
A BIG MINING COMBINE.
Alfred B. Adams, Mexican Mine Pro-
moter, Tells of Rich Ledges.
Alfred B. Adams, who has been in Mex
ico for the past four years endeavoring
to consolidate vast mining properties in
Durango, arrived here yesterday and is
registered at the Occidental. He has
finally succeeded in his efforts, and the re
sult Is the formation of a company called
the Consolidated Mines of Copalquin, of
which he is the director general. He is
here to get bids on an extensive electric
machinery plant with which the mines
will be equipped.
"This company," said Mr. Adams, "has
been incorporated under the laws of New
Jersey and Mexico, and we have letters
patent from the latter Government for
twenty-seven large mines. For two years
I have worked to bring about the consoli
dation of valuable properties, and found
it a difficult task owing to the disinclina
tion of the natives to work their mines
in any other but the crude way. Finally
the De la Rocha family, who own the
larger portion of the claims, agreed to
the combination.
"Principal among the mines is the El
Refugio, which was discovered in 1849.
Even with the primitive methods em
ployed by the natives a fabulous amount
of gold and silver has been extracted.
Over a million dollars in bullion has been
sent to the Mint here during the last five
jcsarsv TUne are C\)jjw .tons of ore in
sight, which runs $27 37 to the ton. It is
free milling ore and the ordinary stamp
mill process is used entirely.
"There are fifteen stamp mills now op
erated by water power. At the junction or
the Limon and Copalquin rivers, which
flow through the properties, we will erect
the electric plant, for which I have come
to this country to negotiate. The com
pany was capitalized at $1,000,000. and the
native owners were given stock to the
value of their holdings. Everything is
now in legal shape under the laws of both
the United States and Mexican Govern
in, nta. I will return to Mexico in three
weeks, and after the machinery has been
constructed 1 may proceed to London and
from various syndicates."

SAN FRANCISCO AND HONGKONGâ
C. T., City. The steamers of the Pacific
Mail line, plying between this city and
Hongkong, usually occupy seventy days
in making the round trip, including stop
page, the longest being ten days at
Hongkong. If the trip is by way of Hon
olulu there is a detention of about twen
ty-four hours at that place, the same at
Yokohama, twelve hours at Kobe, twenty
four hours at Nagasaki, twenty-four
hours at Shanghai and the time stated at
Hongkong.

PROTESTANT AND CATHOLICâ W.
H. X., city. The question, "Are there
more Protestant than Roman Catholic
people in the United States?" is one that
can be answered only if the correspondent
will be more explicit in what he means
by the word "Protestant." The adherents
of Luther are the Protestants in the re
stricted sense, while all Christian denom
inations that differ from the Church of I
Rome are Protestants in the general I
sense. If the correspondent will state, I
what he wants to know the answer will
.be glvca.

WILL MAINTAIN
QUARANTINE
Claims It Represents

RESPONSIBLE TO THE PEOPLE
CONTINUES IN OPPOSITION TO

Secertary Dunne Ordered to Frame *
New ordinance Regulating the
Swab Test in Cases of
Diphtheria.
The Board of Health of this city at a
special and secret meeting on Monday
evening determined that it was the duty
of the board to maintain its quarantine
service. By a recent decision of the Su
perior Court it was held that the Federal
law providing for a fiuarantine service
superseded the State law. This legislated
Dr. Chalmers, the State official, out. of
office.
The court held that the quarantine of
ficial appointed by the Tnited States Gov
ernment had jurisdiction over for
eign vessels entering this port. It
was determined to appeal from this
decision. The members claim they are
held responsible for the health of the
city and that it would not become them
to neglect so potent a factor in the dis
semination of disease germs as vessels
from foreign ports could be. If a plaguo
was spread here the people would hold
the board responsible.
The Quarantine Department will he
maintained r.ntil the matter shall have
been finally adjusted.
The Ma'noncy case was discussed in all
its bearings. 'This is the one in which
it was decided by the court that the or
dinance requiring the swab test in casea
of diphtheria applied to suspected
only and not to those where the, aisease
wa.s known to exist. Secretary Dunne.
was instructed to frame a new ordinance
correcting the defect in the present one.
The members of the board believe the
swab test must be enforced to preserve
the health of the children.

THE. SAN FBANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, 8, 1899.
EFFECTS OF
PRIMARY LAW
IN POLITICS
Subject Being Widely
Discussed.
WILL TEND TOWARD PURITY.
OUTLINE OF THE MOST SALIENT
POINTS.
All Parties Will Be Compelled to
Elect Delegates to Their
Conventions at the
Same Time.
With the announcement in The Call yes
terday that it was the purpose of the
Non-Partisans to have the Stratton prim
ary law declared invalid, if it was within
the range, of possibility, discussion of that
ree'eived a fresh and marked im
petus. Politicians of all parties and of all
b took np the subject and rndeav
ored to ascertain its probahle results, so
far as the local situation Is concerned.
As a general proposition the law was
commended as being conducive to pure
politics and to the destruction of the
power of the bosses.
The law has many new and wide-reach
ing provisions, and differs radically from
the old Porter primary election law, which
it repeals. In order that the' voter may
obtain a general knowledge of this inno
vation in political matters, an outline is
herewith givtn of some of the most sa
lient features of the Stratton law, with
comment here and there to point out
wherein it differs most widely from the
practices heretofore adopted in creating
political conventions.
Should the new primary law withstand
the assaults that are already being
planned against it. it will be the first time
in the history of the United States that
primary elections are made compulsory
to preserve party organizations. This is
considered the most important feature of I
the new statute. It is made absolutely com
pulsory that delegates to party nominating
conventions be elected at primaries to
be held under regulations prescribed by
the law it' the candidates are to retain
their party designations on the ballots.
Heretofore it was at the option of the
various party organizations to hold prim
aries for the selection of delegates or to
appoint them in any manner they might
deem lit. Candidates nominated in any
other manner than by delegates electi 1
at the primary will not be entitli
their party designation on the official
ballot utt-d during the election proper.
Four kinds of primaries are provided
for. based on the political divisions of the
State. State primaries will be held for se
lecting delegates to conventions at which
public officers will be nominated for .
whom the electors of the entire State will !
vote. iMstrlct primaries will be the means
of ( noosing delegates to conventions
at which Congressmen, members of the i
Board of Equalization, Railroad Commis
sioners, Senators or Assemblymen will bo.
nominated. District primaries will be
hi Id for legislative conventions only when
the legislative district comprises" more
than one county. I,ocal primaries will be I
held for delegates to conventions to nor- J
mate candidates for county or city and
county officers, Supervisors, townshirJ
- and legislators representing riis
tricts wholly within one county. City pri
maries will be held for delegates to con- i
ventions which will nominate candidates
for city or town officers. The nominating
thus organized will be known re
spectively as State, district, local and city '.
conventions.
These primary elections are to be con
ducted, managed and controlled as to se
lection ot precinct officers, u.-r of great
registers and supplements thereto, print- i
ing and us. of ballots, ascertainment of
results, and all other details, in the same !
manner as are elections for State and all j
other officers, except as otherwise pro
vided in the law.
Only as many ballots shall be printed
for each primary election precinct as
there are names appearing on the regis
ter or the supplements thereto of persons '
entitled to vote.
No compensation is allowed to precinct
election officers, who will consist of an
Inspector, two judges, two dorks and on.?
ballot clerk for each precinct. No person
shall be compelled to serve as a prlmary
precinct election officer more than once
In two years.
All State, district and local primaries
shall be under the control of the Elec
tion Commissioners of the respective
counties, and shall be a charge on the
funds of the county. City primaries shall
be under the control, of the governing
bodies of the respective cities or towns,
and shall be a charge on the city or
town. Certificates shall be issued to the
ADVERTISEMENTS.

,Mcofla4.lcÂ«mAw^qt-^omeQ^owhr,Qugh^,
elected delegates by the Election Com
missioners or the governing body of the
city or town.
Heretofore primaries were absolutely
controlled by the party holding them, the
precinct officers were usually paid with
political promises, all the expenses of the
primary were paid by the political or
ganization holding the same and the
whclc proceeding was generally viewed as
a farce, played to delude the honest
voter.
All political parties entitled to party
designation on the ballots for the regular !
election are to be entitled to the same j
privileges on the primary election baJlots,
after nling the proper papers with the
Secretary of State. On the governing
committees of the various political parties !
will fall the duty of apportioning- the dele
gates. Should there be any failure to j
make such apportionment by the commit
tee the power is given to the election com
missioners to do so.
State, district and local primaries are to
be held on the second Tuesday of August
of the year In which the election for
State, district or county officers is to be
held. City primaries will be held on the
sixth Tuesday next preceding the election
for city or town officers.
Fifteen days' notice by publication in a
newspaper must be given of the primary
elections.
The balloting will be on the Australian
plan. Names for delegates may be writ
ten on the primary ballot with pen or
pencil, or may be pasted upon it. Voters
can vote for as many delegates as their
party is entitled to in the precinct, but
ballots must nut be marked with the
names of delegates to more than one con
vention. Ballots which show that a voter
has marked a mixed ticket will be disre
garded. All the named marked must be
long to one party.
All persons entitled to vote at a regular
election shall be entitled to vote at the
primary. No other qualification can be
exacted. This does away with the pledge
to support the party ticket, heretofore In
vogue at primaries.
Registration of voters must be made
possible at least sixty-five days before the
â¢primary election.
A majority of the delegates chosen shall
be a quorum cf the convention.
Then to make more emphatic the com
pulsory character of the law there is this
provision, which is considered as a hard
blow at "piece clubs":
"No convention shall be recognized as
in law entitled to make nominations un
less the delegates thereto were elected
under and by virtue of the provisions of
this act. 1 '
Persons chosen to act as primary elec
tion officers who shall willfully fail or re
fuse to act shall be deemed guilty of a
misdemeanor and shall be punished by a
fine of not less than $50 and not more than
Â£200, or by imprisonment for not less than
five days nor more than twenty days, or
by both line and imprisonment. All acts
declared to be offenses against the gen
eral election laws are made offenses
against the primary election law, with
the stme penalties attached.
Chairman A. J. Raisch of the Republi
can County Central Committee was en
thusiastic in his expectations of what the
new primary law is to accomplish in the
vviiv if purifying political methods.
"I believe ;t la a very good thiny." he j
paid, "and will tend to purify political i
practices. It will r>Â»> an incentive to every
citizen to work and vote at the primaries
with the object of having good people rep
resent them at the i onventions.
"As 1 understand this law. the primaries
of all the parties will be held at the same
time and everybody who Is qualified to
vote at all will be permitted to vote at
the primary. This will give the people the
opportunity to select the best men to rep
resent them r.t the conventions, flereto
fore the men e? acted at primaries were put
up by the political tricksters, and the
convi ntlons would be controlled by the
bosses and cliques or a few persons in the
district who were really .t disgrace to the i
people. The primary ticket would be I
made up frequently of persons who were
unknown to the decent voters of the pre
cinct, and in many cases they did not ev< n
reside in the district. Under the new law ,
all this will be changed."
Seth Mann, chairman of the Democratic
State Central Committee, was no less
sparing of good words for the proposed
reform.
"Alter reading the new primary law
over several times." he remarked, "I !
came to the conclusion that it la as good
a system as can be devised for the pur
if a compulsory primary election. !
! The law has been drafted in such a way
as to avoid all thf tloi s which were
; mentioned in the decision of the Supreme
Court when the Stratton primary.
law was declared Invalid. It has been
very carefully drawn and in stub a way
as to preserve all the rights of the differ- ;
ent political parties. It has passed the
close scrutiny of many able lawyers and
of many experienced and astute politi
cians. I think it would be very much to
be regretted if the Supreme Court should
for any reason decide it to be unconsti
; tutional."
"What effect will this new law have on
the local election this full?" he> was
asked.
"It must necessarily depend upon the
political activity of the citizens "of San
: Francisco, Hitherto the complaint has
been made, i id waa justified, that it was
neither pleasant nor safe for citizens to
vote at primaries, but under the protec
tion of this primary law, which provides
, for* the Australian ballot system, it will
be pÂ» fleetly saf>- for citizens to go to the
! booths and cjisU their votes for delegates
Ito the convention of their party. There
fore no public-spirited citizen can longer
have any excuse for not participating in
the primaries."
"What will he the effect on boS3 rule?"
"I think it will tend more than any
thing else." replied Mr. Mann, "to pro
duce a convention representative of the
people of the city, provided the people go
to the polls and vote. If they don't tho
results will be the same as in the past.
The only thing the Legislature can do is
to furnish the means by which the citi
zens can protect themselves against the
machinations of the bosses. If they do
not see fit to take advantage of the law
provided in such cases then they are the
only ones to blame."
Should the Btratton law remain on the
statute book the primary for the first
election under the charter will be held
August 8 and registration will begin on
June 5, or within less than three months
from this time.

EASTERN DRIED FRUIT MARKET.
The New York Commercial says:
"The situation of the raisin market is com
plicated by the uncertainty as to the out
come of the mass-meeting at Fresno, Cal.,
March 1. So far as can be learned, growers
have not begun to nd in their signatures,
and unless an extraordinary change in
policy occurs at the meeting the association
will pass into tory If it does, a large
quantity of raisins will be thrown on tie
market, to be sold for whatever they will
bring and a general tumble In prices is j
likely to result. It is understood that strong \
efforts will be made to prevent such a ruinous |
policy, but it is difficult to see how it ran be !
ptopped. Sultana raisins are in small supply, 1
en<l further importation- are likely to be small.
Trices are now less than the same quality 1
could be brought from London. It is prob
able, judging from present Indications, that 1
tpot' stocks will be cleaned up in a compara- j
tlvely short time.
â â }â¢'â . are steady, under moderate demand.
No further information has been given out
regarding the proposed local combination of |
Oregon Italian interests and the proposition :
has exertc-d no influence on the market as yet. j
"here Is a moderate demand for all sizes, ,
and prices are steady at previously reported !
range
"Apricots are in firm position and scarce.
Conditions indicate that there will likely be
Improvement in prices with Improved demand, I
and occasionally one catches glimpses of what 1
that demand will result in when sales of fancy
pood? an- made at premiums over the present
quotations.
"Of all dried fruits peaches are the strong- â¢

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The storm noted this morning north of Wash
ington has moved rapidly eastward and Is now
central In the British possessions north of
Montana. This depression has caused rain
along the coast as far south as Cape Mendo
cino and In the interior to Red Bluff. The
rainfall In all districts has been very light.
The temperature has fallen over Nevada and
California and risen over Utah.
The pressure Is rising rapidly over Washing
ton, Oregon and Northern California.
Conditions are favorable for lair or partly
cloudy weather in California Wednesday.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight. March 8, 1899:
Northern California Fair Wednesday; cooler;
fresh northwest wind.
Southern California â Fair Wednesday;
cooler north portion; fresh northwest wind.
Nevadaâ Partly cloudy: cooler Wednesday.
Utahâ Partly cloudy Wednesday; probably
rain In north portion.
Arizonaâ Fair Wednesday.
San Francisco and vicinityâ Fair Wednesday;
fresh northwest wind.
G. H. WILLSON, Local Forecast Official.
EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK, March 7.â The stock market
to-day in the final dealings recovered earlier
losses and ended strong and higher than yes
terday. There was an effort on the part of the
professionals to depress prices in the early
dtaliiiK?. which was successful only tempo
rarily to a moderate extent. Investors gener
â¢ ally heiil on to their stock* and the market be
came exceptionally dull. The rate for call
money in the initial transactions touched 'â !%
per cent, with an accompanying easier tone.
Th" exchange market eased oft. with the last
lean at 2' 2 per cent, with a resultant better
tone In the general market. It was said that
because of yesterday's flurry in call money a
c^nsi'lr-rahle amount of unemployed country
bank balances was expected to be transferred
to New York this week. The dealings for
the entire day were less than half a million,
and except for the opening an/1 closing deal
ings the ticker was practically silent at in
tervals. The dealings for London account were
of small volume, with a hardening tendency
at the elope and about the best price* of the
day on the curb. Third-avenue Railroad lost
about 9 points on the public agitation against
the electric lines already on a prominent ave
nue. The grangers, pome other standard
stocks and the Pacifies were even more promi
nent on the rise than in the earlier weakness.
The anthracites, except Lackawanna and Rub
ber, were strung because of the snowstorm and'
cold weather. The forecasts of General
Electrics annual reports being favorable
strengthened that stock. A number of the
specialties, Including People's Gas, rose easily.
Reports that the Chicago and Alton was plan
ning to use Chicago Terminal facilities brought
out speculative' activity in the latter. Th"re
was much difference of opinion on the board re
garding the dividend to-morrow on Sugar with
the general expectation of the regular disburse
ment, and it closed at the best prices
Considerable firmness developed In various
spots In the bond market, but this tendency
was in a measure offset by heaviness in oth
ers. Total sales, 53.145.(X)0. United States 3's
and the old 4's registered declined V and the
new 4's H in the bid price.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, March 7.â There was a show of
strength in wheat at the opening as a result
of firmness at Liverpool on account of reported
damage to the Argentine crop. A restraining
factor was the news from California repeating
yesterday's expression of apprehension con
cerning the winter plant in that State. May
opened %@Vic higher at 72%Â®72%c and touched
72% C but then started to sag. May soon de- j
clined to 72c. When some outside buying or- 1
ders came on the market, however, it caused I
an undercurrent of apprehension concerning I
the winter wheat condition among short sellers,
and in an endeavor to buy back the wheat sold
earlier the price quickly regained the 4c from
the opening decline. Atlantic port clearances
were comparatively small. wheat and (lour
amounting to only 870,000 bushels increase for
the week, whereas a decrease of 1,500,000 bush
els had been expected. This gave the bears the
leverage they needed and the market was vig
orously attacked. May wheat declined to 71 %c,
closing at 71%@71 ! 94c, a loss of 4@%c
Corn showed moderate strength early, but
declined with wheat, closing a shad* lower.
Oats were weak and lower on account of
rather free sales by commission houses against
purchases of corn. May closed with a slight
loss.
Continued light receipts of hogs and large
shipments of meats exerted a strengthening
influence in provisions at the start. The ad
vance was all lost near the close on scattered
selling. Pork and lard are unchanged and
ribs 24c lower.

CALIFORNIA DRIED FRUITS IN THE
EAST.
NEW YORK, March 7.â The Journal of Com
merce says: While there is a good deal of ac
tivity in the market for California dried
fruits and while Stocks here are reported to be
comparatively light for the reason. New York i
Is said to be carrying larger supplies than any j
other market, and as there is reported to be
scarcely anything remaining on the coast and
that Is held at prices relatively higher than |
quotations here, the interior markets are j
' looking to New York for goods. Numerous in
quiries are being received by holders here, we
understand, from large handlers in Philadel
phia. Piltshurg and even Chicago. Not a
small part of the stock they are now anxious
to buy at full prices was, according to re
port bought from them last fall and during j
the winter by New York parties, who had ap
parently more confidence In the market than '
was shown at the time by Western operators
in these goods.
Philadelphia particularly, the report is. sold
a good deal of dried fruit to New York during
the dull season and Is now said to be trying
to buy it back and is offering full market j
prices. The out of town demand seems to be .
chietly for peaches, but there is also consid- !
erable Inquiry for apricots. Stocks of both are :
closely controlled, and holders appear to be In- i
different Fellers, being supported in their po
sition by the brisk and increasing demands of i
local consumers. While interior markets are j
coming to New York for peaches and apricots,
buyers here are said to be seeking stocks of
prunes in other cities. Of fifteen or twenty
cars bought here last week by a prominent
Jobbing house the report is, that a part of it
was purchased from Chicago firms, and it is
rumored that negotiations are pending on
other lots held in that market. The consum
ing demand for prunes, as well as other dried
fruits, excepting raisins, has shown marked
improvement during the past week and Job
bers are displaying greater interest than at
any time since the fall season closed. Car
load orders are more frequent and the bulk of ;
business is in smaller quantities. The demand I
is not of a speculative character, the stock for â 
the most part going into immediate consump
tion. Notwithstanding the increased demand, â¢
prices do not appreciably advance, though the i
market is firm and the tendency upward.

LONDON WOOL SALES.
LONDON, March 7. â The second series of thÂ»
) C T''( wool auction sales opened to-day: The of- j
ferings numbered 11.134 bales, largely cross- I
bred.", none of which was suitable for America
and little fit for Continental uses. The home
trade secured the bulk. Medium low grades
were a farthing to a half penny below the
January average. Scoured merinos were
scarce and in good demand. Queensland and
South Australia greasy merinos brought full
prices. There was a good attendance.

THE S'iOUK MARKET.
Hawaiian Commercial led the market for local
securities, advancing to $7S 50. The other stcx-ks
showed little change.
Mining stocks were generally firmer on the
morning session, but weakened off again.
Water is being poured into the Consolidated
California and Virginia mine to put out the old
flre, as will be seen by the following telegram
from the pump: "Water twenty-seven feet be
low Die 1750-foot level. The increased raise is
due to the BUrface water being turned into the
Consolidated California slopes at the 1650-foot
level."
H<-st & Belcher and Occidental are assessed
10 eentM.
The Yellow Jacket Mining Company has
levied an assessment of 1"> rents per sljare.
The delinquent assessment sale of the Julia
Consolidated Mining Company will be held to
day.
The Pennsylvania Mtning Company of Grass
Valley has declared its twenty seventh divi
dend, at the rate of f, cents per share, amount
ing to $2.'f*i, payable Immediately.
The Vallejo Quicksilver Mining Company of
Napa County has levied an assessment of 2
cents per share, delinquent March 20.
In the Utah Consolidated mine during the
past week the northwest drift, started at a
point in the main west drift, 120 feet In, was
advanced fourteen feet In porphyry, with a
streak of quartz six inches wide assaying $8
per ton officially reported to have been cut.
The total length of the northwest drift Is
thirty feet.
The Pri ific Gas Improvement Company and
the San Jose Water Company will pay the
usual monthly dividends of 50 cents per share
on the loth.
The California Street (ab:e Company will
pay a monthly dividend of 50 cents per share
on the usual date this month.
The Bunker Hill and Sullivan Mining Com
pany of Idaho paid a dividend of $21,000 on
the 4th.
The dividend of the Morning Star mine of
California this month Is $3 per share.
The directors of the Occidental Consolidated
have resolved to send a communication to the I
presidents of the Chollar and Potosl mining ]
companies submitting â  Proposition to resume I
work In thÂ» so-called "Zadlg drift," on the j
Brunswick lode, at joint expense, and continue
the same through the ground of the Chollar and
Potosl until it reaches the Occidental line. It
is estimated that the expense of extending the
drift an additional 1000 fp^t. or to the Occi
dental north line, will not exceed $10,000.

NOTE â the above exposition â of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column, and the successive tides of the
: day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column Rives the second tide of .
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day, except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
I given are additions to the soundings on the
i United States Coast Survey charts, except
l when a minus sign ( â precedes the height,
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low waters.

YELLOV; TAIL BEATEN AGAIN
GOLDEN RULE LED FOSTER'S
CRACK OUT.
Imperious Scored Again for Pat
Dunne â Lavator Presented With
a Purse â Mary Black Cap
tured the Handicap.
Fred Foster's high-priced youngster,
Yellow Tail, again suffered defeat at In- j
pleside yesterday. He was one of eleven
starters in the three and a half furlong '
scramble for two-year-olds, and after get
ting away well was beaten through the I
ttrttch by Golden Rule. The latter, a 2 !
to 5 favorite, was knocked to his knees at
the start, by Florentii and then won com
fortably.
It was a rare good day for favorites. !
One after another thundered down the
l'.ne, until it came to the last event, when \
a halt was called. O'Connell, the vete- !
ran, an fc to 5 chance, finished third.
The defeat of O'Connell was not entirely ;
unexpected. The old horse was asked to
carry 123 pounds ana down such fast ones '
as Mary Black, Gualala and Mldllght. He |
chased Gualala for a way and then gave i
it up. Mary Black, ridden by Johnny
Keiff, and 7 and S to 1 in the betting, won I
easily from the light-weighted Good Hope.
With a capable rider on her back. Louis i
Ezell'a good filly Espionage showed to ]
much better advantage in the opening i
mile run, decided under selling conditions.
Starting a 7 to 5 favorite, with Hennessy
up, she passed Saintly before the stretch
was reached, and in a drive led Racivan
cut a head. The latter was heavily backed j
at the last minute, and though Rutter
rode a nice race on the gelding she could
not get up.
The third event, a Fix furlong sprint,
caw Lavator enjoy a cake-walk, coming
home in rag- time. The Jennings entry \
ruled an odds-on favorite, and won from
Winifred, with Spencer easing up. So- j
â  kombeo took the show.
Barney Sohrieber's Aluminum only cap- !
tured the live and a half furlong dash j
that followed by th narrowest of mar
gins. The ling, with Devln up, was an |
even money favorite, and after leading !
into the stretch just lasted long enough j
to earn the verdict from Sohnitz by a
nose. Midas beat two other cheap platers i
out for the show.
Imperious, at odds of even money, took
the seven furlong selling affair in a hard
drive from Benamela. Zamar ran a bad
â  third.

picking- all" six winners.
Eddie Jones and Spencer will not be seen '
in the saddle much at Ingleside. They do !
not fancy the rulings made by the stand.

AN EARLY FIRE.
Nearly Four Thousand Dollars Worth
of Damage Done on Fourth
Street.
A fire broke out at half-past 5 o'clock ,
ve=terrtay morning in the rear of the Sun
meat market on Fourth street near .
Mission. The Fire Department had the [
flames under control in about fifteen mm- ,
utrs. but considerable damage -was done.
The butcher shop of Robert
marhor In the rear of which tne nre
â¢rt.d wns damaged $300 worth, fully
. red by Insurance. Co-operative Gro
v 110 Fourth street, $1000 damage, in
â l. Mrs. Ann Lawrence s lodging
house over the grocery was damaged
in insurance. R. Munk's printing es
tablishment and residence sustained a loss
of $500. W. I. Matthews, watchmaker, ,
and David Braid. Btraw bat maker, were j
fi:tmap.il by water. j
All the buildings except that at 110
Fourth street are owned by v illiam ir

Carelessness in trying out lard in the,
rear of the butcher shop was the cause I
of the fire. This is the fourth accidental
'. fire in the sanv; place in three months.

TWO NATURAL BORN FIGHTERS.
_ /-> EORGE ("KID") T^AVIGNE, the Saginaw welter-weight champion, and "Mysterious Billy" Smith, the champion of ||
O I East port, Me., will meet on Friday evening in Woodward's Pavilion to battle for the championship of the class Q
',*> I T they represent. Jim" McDonald will referee the bout. Lavigne is a slight favorite in the betting because of the Â»*
Â£ *â* fact that lie has never suffered a defeat. Smith, although a hero of many hard-fought battles, has some black &
Â§ marks on his record, or, in other words, contests which were decided against him because of his then foul style of fight- p.
;â¢ Ing. It is said, however, and on good authority, that In the last few years he has been conducting himself according to ci
<> rule and that he now finds the road to success is to be gained only by closely adhering to Queensberry requirements. Â£>
*â * The pugilists have trained most carefully for this coming contest, as both are well aware of the fact that success will *'
O. mean a great feather in the hat of the winner. .-0
Â«* Lavigne, as previously stated in The Call, has been stationed at his old favorite quarters, the Six Mile House, 2*
'&. since the day on which articles were signed. John L. Sullivan and "Jake" Kilrain were impressed with his appearance JO
88 when they visited him last week. Sullivan said that he was the greatest bit of human fighting flesh that he had ever jj?
Or seen, and predicted his success on Friday evening. jO
2$ "But, young fellow," blustered the retired champion heavy-weight, ""you must not forget that you will have a fight gi
O on your hands, because this fellow Smith is a whirlwind, and doesn't know actually when he is licked, for ho never lets <0
V up on his opponent." â¢ J?
% According to the articles of agreement, the men must weigh in at 142 pounds. Smith will not be one ounce under H
q that weight, but Lavigne expects to enter the ring at 138 pounds. He will therefore be handicapped four pounds, and Â£j
ft so far as height and reach are concerned Smith will have a great advantage, as he is five Inches taller and three inches g?
O longer in the reach. Â£l

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, MARCH 8, 1899.
THE LOVE OF
A SAILOR HAS
TURNED TO HATE
Duerrbeck Courted a
Married Woman.
LAVISHED MONEY ON HER
JOSEPHINE BARTLETT'S DECEIT
GETS HER IN TROUBLE.
A Warrant Is Now Out for Her Ar
rest on a Charge of Obtaining
Money by False Pre
tenses.
That "the course of true love never j
runs smooth" has been again exempli- :
fied in the case of W. T. Duerrbeck and j
Josephine Bartlett, and instead of being i
happily married, as Duerrbeck fondly ex- j
they would bo, he has now asked '
the aid of the law to send Josephine to
San Quentin on a felony charge.
Duerrbeck is a seafaring man and lives
at 133 East street. He was of a saving
disposition and accumulated wealth. Two !
years ago he met Josephine in a dance |
hall and was attracted by her appear- |
ance. He frequented the dance hall sev- j
era! times and spent .his money lavishly â 
so that Josephine might earn a good com
mission. Finally they agreed to dwell to- '
gether and he furnished a nice home for I
her. On his return from one of his trips
he thought he had reason to be jealous
and the home was broken up and so was
the furniture.
The sailor's love for Josephine hurned |
as fiercely as ever and a reconciliation '
was effected. Josephine expressed a <I< -
sire to keep a rooming- house, as it would !
not be so lonesome for her when he was
at sea, and October 20 last he pave her ;
$450 to purchase the lodging-house at 230
O'Farrell street. She paid the money to.
Mrs. Eliza Stokes, the landlady, and took '
possession of the house.
All this time Josephine had led Duf>rr
beck to believe that she was an unmar
ried woman and would marry him when
he named the happy day. It was agreed
that after he returned from his next trip
to Alaska the marriage would take place.
He arrived a few days ago and. procur
ing the marriage license, went to 2*)
O'Farrell street and was surprised to find
that Josephine had sold out the place and
left the city.
On making inquiry he ascertained that
Josf-phine at the time he met her had a
husband. Albert E. Bartlett. He came to
the conclusion that she had swindled him
and pla'-ed the matter in the hands of his
attorneys. The result was that yesterday,
accompanied by his lawyers, he waited ;
upon Judge Mogan and swore to a com
plaint charging Josephine with a felony
In obtaining money by false pretenses.
Altogether he said hn had squandered !
about $4000 on Josephine and her whims.
The woman In the case is at present in j
Ran Jose and the warrant was sent to |
Chief Kidward with instructions to arrest !
her As soon as she is apprehended a de- |
tective will be sent to bring her back to
the city.
Not the Murderer.
LoulÂ© Lung, the Chinese arrested Mon- \
day by Detectives Wren and McMahon
on suspicion of having kicked to death
Quong Guey, the slave girl, at 23Mj Ross
alley, was released from custody yester
day morning. Louie was able to prove
conclusively that he had nothing to do
with the murder and had never been in
hiding, as was alleged.
PARDON' FOR JOHNSON.
Effort Being Maae on Behalf of a
Convicted Counterfeiter.
A petition is in circulation asking the
Governor to pardon Thomas Johnson, now
serving a seven years' sentence in the
San Quentin Penitentiary for count
ing. ? Johnson has already served two
years or his sentence. It is represented
that he has a wife and two young chil
dren dependent upon him for support;
that the crime of which he was convict
ed was his first offense and that he has
been already sufficiently punished.
Ex-Assistant United States Attorney
Schlesinger prosecuted Johnson and has
signed the petition. It will be presented
to United States Circuit Judge Morrow
for his signature to-day.
Double Tracks to Holy Cross.
Superintendent Clayton of the San
Francisco and San Mateo Electric Rail
way Company broke ground yesterday on
Dolores and Chenery streets preparatory
to laying a double track from Thirtieth
and Guerrero streets to Holy Cross Ceme
tery. The company has but recently fin
ished laying a double track on its Eigh
teenth-street western branch leading to
Golden Gate Park.
The Holy Cross line will be constructed
very substantially, and the company will
hurry the rebuilding of the long line
through the two counties, and may event
ually go direct to the town ot Sun Mateo
via Baden.

', INCREASE OP HEART TROUBLES.
âº _

â  j Americans, are certainly increasing, and
I I while this may be largely due to the ex-
I citement and worry of American busi-
| ness life it is more often the result of
weak stomachs, of poor digestion.
Real organic disease is Incurable; but
not one case in a hundred of heart
trouble Is organic.
The close relation between heart trouble
and poor digestion is because both organs
are controlled by the same great nerves,
the Sympathetic and Pneumogastric.
In another way also the heart is affect-
ed by the form of poor digestion, which
causes gas and fermentation from half-
digested food. There is a feeling of op-
pression and heaviness in the chest,
caused by pressure of the distended stom-
ach on the heart and lungs, lnterefering'
with their action; hence arises palpita-
tion and short breath.
Poor digestion also poisons the blood,
making it thin and watery, which irri-
tates and weakens the heart.
The most sensible treatment for heart
trouble is to improve the digestion and
I to insure the prompt assimilation of food.
This can be done by the regular use

ALAMEDA COUNTY NEWS.
SOLID SIX
MAY YET BE
A-SCHEMING
Its Low Ordinance Is
Not Favored.
WAITING FINAL PASSAGE
ITPTON'S FIGURES MAY NOT
BE ACCEPTED.
After the Election Some of the Pres
ent Peculiarities May Be Made
Clear â Doubt of Good
Faith.
Oakland Office* San Francisco Call,
Broadway, 7.
Will the "Solid Six" remain dieinteg- j
rated when the water rate ordinance
come? up for final passage, or are they
merely "playing 'possum" until after the
election to hoip out the only two of their
number who are candidates for re-elec
(Vatkinson and Woodward? A com- j
parlson of the ordinance passed to print j
last night by the Council with the ordi- |
nance introduced by Mr. Earl and the old
ordinance prepared by ex-Councilman
Manuel shows that Earl and Manuel agree
In every particular. In fact, Mr. Earl, for
reasons best known to himself, merely
copied 0â¢â  old Manuel ordinance and j
handed it in to the Council. Mr. Upton's j
ordinance, which was. passed, is far more
generous to ;he water company than that
of the Juanuel-Eurl ordinance, but the I
gentlemen who framed it declare that it j
is only |ust to the corporation and not
too high. Four years ago. when Manuel j
was in the Council, representing the same ;
element that is now being represented by [
the consistent five Councilmenâ Cuvellier,
Upton, Bowe, Girard and Pringleâ he pre- \
pared his ordinance after much research. |
There was at that time a desire to run j
things to extremes, and the ordinance |
would not have paid any dividends on j
Btock had it been in force. This has been i
true although the ordinance was never en
forced, rate-cutting commencing between
the rival companies soon after its passage.
Ten days must elapse before the ordi
nance comes up for final passage. Last
night three members of the "six" went
to the other side and sustained I pton s
ordinance. Earl, Watkinson and Broana
han alone voted for the Manuel-Earl or
dinance which is about one-half or per- I
haps one-third of the schedule favored
by those three gentlemen last year. In
the interval the election will take place,
and after that all members of the Council
will breathe more freely and feel more
comfortable. . I
Just what part the water monopoly is
taking in the fixing of rates and in the i
present campaign is not very clear. While
there is a need of a high rate to enable if|
to float the newly issued bonds and stock,
there is no desire to give the business a
black eye in the money market by raising ,
a riot on account of "dealing" with the
City Council. With the two companies
United, operating expenses will be largely
reduced, and it would seem that a lower
rate could therefore be levied. The situa- j
tion just now is ludicrous. The men cred-
Ited with being tied to the corporation
have introduced figures considerably lower
than those of the other element In the
Council, and yet the figures of Mr. Earl ,
are precisely those of the predecessors
In the Council of "the five. The ordinance
wfll come up for final passage in two
weeks, and then it will be seen what kind
of a trickâ if a trick at allâ the Solid {
Bix" is trying to perform.
CONDUCTOR PELTON
KILLED BY A TRAIN
STEPS DIRECTLY IN FRONT OF A
LOCOMOTIVE.
He Is Badly Crushed and Dies WhilÂ»
Being Removed to tho<Fabiola
Hospital.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
Broadway, March 7.
George E. Pelton, a conductor on the
/laywards local train, was si ruck by the j
engine of the Oakland ' local at Brooklyn;
station at 9:iJO this morning and so badly
Injured that he died while being removed I
to the Fabiola Hospital. Pelton left his j
home at 1418 Eignth avenue a few mm- i
uus after 9 o'clock, intending to catch the j
9:30 train to the pier. As the train pulled ,
into the station Pelton. for some reason :
that can be explained only on the theory j
of absent-mindedness, stepped out on the
track directly in front of the advancing ,
engine. He was struck by the cylinder of
the locomotive and knocked down, falling
beneath the steps of the forward coach. '
He was picked up in an unconscious con
dition badly crushed about the body and
Euffering from a number of severe bruises ,
about the head. The unfortunate man i
was put aboard the train and taken to the j
Broadway station, where an ambulance ]
from Fabiola Hospital was in waiting, but
expired while en route to that institution \
and the remains were taken to the I
morgue.
Deceased had been employed by the I
Southern Pacific Company most of the
time as a conductor for fifteen years, and
was regarded as one of its most trusted
employes. He was extremely popular j
with the patrons of the road, and his un
timely death will be deeply regretted. He â 
was 49 years old and leaves h lamily con
sisting of a widow, mother, a daughter
13 years old and a widowed daughter with
a little child. He was a member of the
Brotherhood of Railway Conductors,
which organization will have charge of
the funeral arrangements. The remains
will be interred in the family plat In Mon
terey.
Coroner Mehrmann and a jury Inquired
into Pelton's death to-night, and found
a verdict of accidental death. The jury
also exonerated from all blame the crew I
of the Seventh-street local, which struck
Pelton. Deceased was a native of Wis
consin, aged 49 year?.
DEMOLISHED THE FURNITURE
James Gregory Wrecks His Homa
Witn an Ax.
OAKLAND, March 7â James Gregory,
an upholsterer, residing at 1647 Market
street, had a dispute with his wife this
morning anl proceeded to get even by j
demolishing his household effects with an
ax. Mrs. Gregory escaped from the house
while the work of demolition was going
cm and rushing to the City Hall swore
to a complaint charging her husband with
disturbing the peace. When Officer Cur
rlss arrived on the scene with the war
rant Gregory had barricaded the doors
and locked himself in the basement. The
officer finally forced an entrance, but
Gregory fled through the back door and
tried to escape. He was overhauled after
an exciting chase over fences and through
the gardens of neighbors and then
showed fight, but was quickly subdued.
Every stick of furniture in the house had
been reduced to kindling wood. Kren the
family album had been hacked to pieces
by the enraged man. Gregory was re
cently arrested on a charge of drunken
ness and judgment was suspended during
his good behavior. He will probably be
severely dealt with for his latest out
break.

DEMOCRACY
IS WONDERING
WHERE IT IS
Is Repudiated by Its
Own Organ.
CAPTAIN HACKETT MOVES
A SECRET CONVENTION STIRS
UP MUCH INTEREST.
Auditor Snow Returns to the City
and Will Attend Personally
to His Campaign for
i- Mayor.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, March 7.
Oakland has never had such a campaign
as she Is now enduring. Never before hai.
everything been so completely mixed up
and party lines po far stretched. The most
interesting row is among the Democracy.
This party, which has generally been in
the minority, does not quite know where
it is Without Its knowledge a few of its
leaders put up a proposition several
months ago to indorse the candidates of
the Municipal League, provided that some
of the candidates were Democrats. The
league met, and being more anxious for
Republican support than Democratic sup
port it nominated none but Republicans.
The Democrats started out to make a
campaign for themselves and found to
their sorrow that the only Democratic or
gan around the bay was against them.
It had decided to support the Republican
ticket, and but for the kindly feeling of a
local newspaper the Democracy might
have made a campaign and no one have
been the wiser. Not only has their organ
served the Democracy with notice that it
can expect no favors but it Is ignoring
iht-m entirely, and although Beth Mann,
chairman of the State Central Democratic
Committee, and Hon. W. W. Foote have
taken part in the campaign their names
have neyer appeared in the party's organ.
After declaring tnat he would not make
any kind of a light for the office of City
Auditor, Captain Ed Hackett, the Demo
cratic nominee, has changed his mind and
he started oui to-day to decorate the town
-with red and black posters. This has add
ed interest to th<- contest for this office.
A. H. Breed, the Republican nominee,
will draw the full vote of his party, while
D. C. Hinsdale will share the balance of
the vote with Captain Hackett.
Much curiosity has been shown regard
ing a secret convention which was held
on the l^th of February at Hamilton Hall.
It has been generally understood that no
issues outside of purely political ones
should be brought into this campaign, and
the candidates are anxiously working to
discover, if possible, what was done, at
â ret conclave.
R. W. Snow, the Republican nominee
for Mayor, is now actively conducting his
own campaign. He reports the outlook
as being very favorable to the whole He
publican ticket, and expects to see a
sweeping victory next Monday.
SECRET MARRIAGES ARE
IN VOGUE IN ALAMEDA
LUCY O'BRIEN ASTOUNDS HER
FRIENDS.
She Was Wedded Last November in
a Romantic Manner and Now
Confesses the Fact.
ALAMEDA, March 7.â Alameda is de
veloping a wealth of clandestine mar- \
riag-es. Following close upon the heels of
the Shane-Homeier romance comes an
other love affair, ending in a wedding in
which James Connely of Petaluma and
Miss Lucy O'Brien of this city were the
principal figures.
Connely has been paying court to Miss
O'Brien for nearly a year, but the mother
of the young lady objected to the Peta
luman as a son-in-law and forbade him to
visit the O'Brien home. However, early
last November the couple met in San
Francisco by appointment and were mar
ried by a Justice of the Peace. The bride
returned to her Alameda home and kept
her secret. A few days ago the husband
secured employment at the Presidio in j
San Francisco and sent word to his wife
that he was prepared to provide for her, i
and suggested that it was about time that j
she should place herself under his pro
tecting care.
Without consulting her mother the ;
young lady packed a few of her belong
ings and joined her husband. As soon as
Mrs. O'Brien discovered her daughter' a
absence she jump'-'i at th>- conclusion that
there had been an elopement in the fam- :
ily and did everything she could to appre
hend the runaway. .Later Mrs. O'Brien re- |
ceived a letter from the young lady di
vulging the secret wedding and stating
that she and her husband were living to
gether happily in San Francisco. Mrs.
O'Brien has not yet recovered from the
shock.
This is not the first experience of the
kind in the O'Brien family. All of the
daughtersâ three in number â have sur
rounded their matrimonial affairs with a
tinge of romance. One eloped to Los An
geles, where she subsequently died. An
other, who was a teacher in the Alameda
public schools, was secretly married to a
commercial traveler from Oregon. She
continued teaching until she was forced
to resign her position, when her marriage
announcement was published. Pubs>-
quently she joined her husband and is now
living in the East. Miss Lucy's escapade
makes the career of the young ladies
completely and truly romantic.
Said He Was Held Up.
OAKLAND, March 7.â John Wood of
East Oakland reported to the police "to
night that he was held up by two well
dressed strangers near Twelfth and
Oak streets about 10 o'clock and relieved
of $60 in gold. He had on his person also
three watches, which the footpads did not
disturb. The police are inclined to dis
credit Wood's story.

THE PA!N" FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY", MARCH 8, 1899.
INVADED CELL
OF AN ACCUSED
MURDERESS
Sensation Sprung in
Mrs. Brandes' Case.
CHARGES BY HER ATTORNEY
SAWYER AVERS THAT OFFICERS
ASKED HER QUESTIONS.
William A. Brandes' Trial Delayed
Through the Sudden Illness
of One of the Jurors,
Philip Sieben.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, March 7.
Attorney F. W. Sawyer endeavored to
create a sensation in Judge Hall's depart
ment to-day, when Mrs. Etta Brandes,
charged with murder, appeared to have |
i her ca.se continued another week on ac- i
j count of the trial of her husband, Wil- |
I liam A. Brandes, occupying the court's j
I attention.
Attorney Sawyer, addressing Judge
; Hall, stated that since the adjournment j
in Brandes' trial last Friday it had come
to his knowledge that two officers had ap-
I proached Mrs. Brandes in the County
j Jail and had attempted to extort ad
i missions from her, notwithstanding she
I had been advised by her attorneys not j
!to be Interviewed. He said that these offl- j
cers represented that they were author
ized to do so by the court, and he asked
j Judge* Hall to protect his client from such ;
; unfair methods.
The statement created a considerable |
stir momentarily. Judge Hall expressed \
surprise, and assured Mr. Sawyer that he
bad authorized no une to ask Mrs. j
Brandes any questions, and moreover i
that he would In no case do so.
Sawyer subsequently stated that Police
man Bert Curtis and Deputy Sheriff
George V. Taylor had invaded Mrs.
Brandes' cell, insisting that she an
swer questions as to her age, nativity,
the date of her marriage and even her ;
i weight. "The officers threatened," con
tinued Sawyer, "that they would find a I
way to compel her to answer if she re- j
fused." Mrs. Brandes emphatically re
fused to answer any of the questions.
George Taylor is also assistant jailer, ]
and when questioned by a Call reporter |
easily explained Sawyer's charges. He
seemed greatly amused over the accusa
tions.
"1 have to admit," said Taylor, "that '
we went to Mrs. Brandes' celL We did !
i ask her the questions, but it was abso
lutely in our line of duty. We were seek-
I ing information for the rogue's gallery,
i We wished to get an accurate descrip
tion of the prisoner for our records. It is
an absurd hulabaloo to suppose that we
1 had any thought of using such informa
tion at the woman's trial."
Mrs. Brandes' case was continued for
i another week, and then the trial of her j
â  accused husband was resumed, the de- |
â  ailing to the stand Dr. A. H. l'rutt
ias a medical expert, whose testimony
! briefly -was to the effect that evidences
lof hanging in the way of superlicial
marks about the neck are not prominent
in cases of young persons where the liga
ture is removed soon after death.
j Though blood- oozed thirty-one hours af- I
! ter death and a parchment skin appeared
, on the neck, the expert thought, these
would not necessarily indicate hanging.
In reply to a question put by Juror i
i Sieben Dr. Pratt admitted that author
i ities on evidences of hanging are so con
tradictory that there is no accounting for
them.
on cross-examination Dr. Pratt testi- :
: fled that there were adhesions and mi
i flammation in the dura mater of Lillian >
> Brandes that were abnormal. As to me
mark on tin dead girl's neck, witness said
It might have been made by the apron In
evidence, provide. i it was twisted into a
very firm condition, such as a rope.
The trial was here brought to a sudden |
termination for the day by reason of one :
of- the jurors. Philip Sieben, being taken '
sick. Sieben, who resides in Alameda, |
had been ill this morning when he came
: over. He .suffered from flfzzlness and a
rushing of blood to the head, and his nose
began bleeding profusely. Accordingly )
court adjourned until 0:30 to-morrow
morning. _^_____ - __ ââ _
UNIVERSITY OF
CALIFORNIA NEWS
President Harper's Visit â Lieutenant
Hayne No Longer Exploring the
Philippines.
'BERKELEY, March 7.â Dr. William T.
Harper, president of the University of
Chicago, who has accepted an Invitation
to deliver the public address at the uni
versity Charter day celebration on March
23, will arrive in Berkeley Tuesday, March
21. It is expected that his visit to the
coast will not extend over seven days.
As far as can be learned now he intends
to divide his time between California and
Stanford. On Wednesday before Char
ter day he will deliver a public address be
fore the whole student body. On Charter
day, after speaking at the commemora
tion exercises by Founders' Rock, on the
college campus, he is to be the guest of
honor at a university banquet to be
given the same evening in San Fran
cisco. Friday morning the distinguished
visitor will go to Stanford, returning
to Oakland in time to address a large
public gathering there Sunday after
noon.
A committee consisting of William T
Mooney of California and R. A. Leiter of
Stanford have made the final arrange
ments for the next intercollegiate debate
The contest Itself is to come off In Met
ropolitan Temple, San Francisco, on the
night of April 22. Both colleges have
agree.d upon the following subject for dis
cussion: "Resolved, That the retention
of the Philippines is contrary to the prin
ciples for which this Government should
stand." Stanford has chosen the afllrm
ati%'e of the question, leaving the nega
tive to California.
The senior class, which graduates next
May, has petitioned the Board of Re
gents to refund $5 of the amount which
each member was required to deposit on
entering college for the customary dl
ploma fee. Previously the fee was $10,
but since 1897 the board has reduced the
sum by one-half. A similar petition was
presented by the class graduating last
year, and being granted by the regents,
each member way returned half the
amount originally deposfted. The pres
ent class expects the same favorable an
swer to their request, and part of the
sum, if obtained may go toward defray
ing the expenses for class day exercises
at the close of the term.
Miss Berg, Miss Rothermel, Miss Lulu
Simpson, Earl Swan and Roscoe Logan
have been appointed a committee to ar
range for a senior reception to be given
on Wednesday evening, March 15.
A letter has been received from Lieu
tenant Arthur P. Hayne. formerly of the
agricultural department of the unner
sity but now in active service in Ma
nila, stating that his work as agricul
tural expert for the Government had
been suspended temporarily. Lieutenant
Hayne was detached from the regular
army by orders from Washington and
commissioned to conduct an exploration
over the Philippines for the purpose of
forwarding a detailed report to the Bu
reau of Agriculture at Washington. The
work progressed very favorably for a
time, but the attitude' of the natives grew
so hostile that no further progress could
be gained. It was decided to suspend ex
ploration for the time being, and Lioulen
ant Hayne is now aid on the staff of Gen
eral Anderson.
